                             MEETING ANNOUNCEMENT

                        The Portland Linux/Unix Group
                                  will meet
                          7 PM Thursday Jan 4, 2007
                                      at
                          Portland State University
                                      in
                            Smith Memorial Center
                                   Room 294
                           On the block bounded by
       SW Montgomery, SW Broadway (7th), SW Harrison, and SW Park (9th)

      *******************************************************************

                                 PRESENTATION

                                      by

                              Randal L. Schwartz
                           <merlyn at stonehenge.com>


      As most of you know Randal is "the primary person to blame
      for Stonehenge Consulting Services, Inc."  He is author of
      many of the principal books and articles on Perl programming,
      and gives many workshops including workshops with Geek Cruises.
      Randal is also an expert on computer crime both as a consultant
      to law enforcement agencies and as a defendant who became
      a felon for doing his job with a bit too much enthusiasm.

      I'm waiting for Randal to verify the topic of his talk, but
      I believe he intends to talk about his experiences with
      computer crime and the legal system and what you need to do
      to avoid having similar problems.

      *******************************************************************

          Agenda:

              7:00 - 7:30  Business
                   We will discuss the status of our ongoing projects
                   including PLUG's monthly Advanced Topics meetings,
                   PLUG's monthly hands on clinics, PLUG for Education,
                   etc.

              7:30 - 8:30  Presentation

                   See above

              9:00 - ...  Beer
                                  The Lucky Labrador Brewing Company
                                  915 SE Hawthorne



                                          David Mandel
                                          Chief Activist
                                          Portland Linux/Unix Group
                                          560 SE Alexander
                                          Corvallis, Oregon 97333
                                          (541) 730-5285 mobile
                                          dmandel at pdxLinux.org


          P.S.  Biznix which specializes in Linux and UNIX support for
                the Business Computer User.
                As I understand, they haven't been meeting for a while,
                but intend to have regular meetings again in 2006.
                See http://www.Biznix.org for details.

          P.S.  BLUEgroup (The Beaverton Linux Users Education Group)
                To the best of my knowledge, BLUEgroup no longer meets.
                See www.BLUEgroup.org for details.

          P.S.  PDXLUG provides a less formal alternative to PLUG
                emphasizing the needs of new users.   PDXLUG meets
                every second Thursday of the month in southeast Portland.
                See http://www.PDXLUG.org for details.

          P.S.  The Mid Willamette Valley Linux Users Group meets
                at 6:30 PM on the first Tuesday of the month.
                They have out grown their meeting location at PEAK
                and are now meeting on the OSU campus.
                See http://www.lug.corvallis.or.us/ for details.

          P.S.  The Eugene Linux Users Group meets several times a month.
                See http://www.euglug.org for details.

Subject: [PLUG] Ubuntu standard Firefox sucks

Under Kunbuntu I installed Firefox using Adept_Manager.
I could not even get Firefox to go to google.com or yahoo.com.
Originally I thought is was my routing table, but no, it was Firefox.
I started looking for a 2.0 version in .deb form.
I finally found a .deb in SwiftFox, which is an optimized Firefox.

Downloaded it and installed (after removing Firefox) and everything seems
happy.
Why is Ubuntu distributing a broken Firefox?

From johnxj at comcast.net  Tue Jan  2 00:00:01 2007
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Tue, 2 Jan 2007 00:00:01 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Ubuntu standard Firefox sucks
In-Reply-To: <006e01c72e1a$a652a550$6d01a8c0@RAMA>
References: <006e01c72e1a$a652a550$6d01a8c0@RAMA>
Message-ID: <20070102000001.ee2c11e3.johnxj@comcast.net>

On Mon, 1 Jan 2007 19:03:58 -0800
"William A Morita" <wamorita at hevanet.com> dijo:

> I finally found a .deb in SwiftFox, which is an optimized Firefox.
> 
> Downloaded it and installed (after removing Firefox) and everything seems
> happy.
> Why is Ubuntu distributing a broken Firefox?

My understanding is that SwiftFox is proprietary. Ubuntu is big on not
including anything in its standard repositories that is not completely
open source. If you want stuff like that you must add the restricted
repositories.

As for why 2.0 is not available, that is because things in the debian
world are slow. It's even worse in 64-bit Ubuntu.

I should also add that my Firefox 1.5.0.8 runs just fine on my Dapper
amd-64 computer, aside from some missing plugins.

Message-ID: <1167684094.30198.96.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>

> >From a managment standpoint DHCP is much easier but requires more upfront
> work (DHCP server, Accounting/Radius). If you're using a static setup you
> risk having only roughly 63 customers per /24 subnet (depending on how its
> engineered) so you're looking at additional cost as you grow because you're
> paying someone to submit applications to ARIN for new subnets as well as
> paying the registration fee's. Of course if you go DHCP then you have to
> deal with things like the MPAA and RIAA complaints and being able to
> accurately correlate authentication and IP data to a specific account 
> which takes time and money :-)
> 
> This idea could be argued for ever and ever but I guess comes down to the
> time and money and engineering you want to put into a network

Well, DSL service providers offer static subnets where they don't do
DHCP for those customers.  With the advent of IPV6, ip addresses should
get cheaper.  As far as arguing that you can only put 255 people on
a /24 and that this isn't profitable, we are talking about DSL customers
or cable customers that are always supposed to have access.  If you only
have 1 subnet of 255 hosts and more than that many customers, say three
times as many, probably only a third of your customer can use the
service simultaneously.  Considering people tend 
to want access at the same time, 66% on average are probably going to be
angry when they get blocked.  There used to be dial-up ISP's that hit
13:1 ratios for people trying to get on.  People don't expect a
broadband experience to be like a dial-up one.  Besides that, there
probably aren't any dial-up ISP's that are that bad anymore.

The average DSL customer pays a minimum of $25/month.  That is $1575
gross from 255 paying customers each month.  Now what does the OC-3
feeding these people cost?  How much is lost to taxes?  $50+/month is
common for bridged customers with a subnet that have decent bandwidth.
The fees to ARIN, not an issue for ISP's because they already bridge
customers without doing dhcp and charge the customer for it in the
process.

Dynamic assignment of ip addresses only means you can serve more
customers if you are lucky enough to find people that are willing to
time share.  Considering that most people want Net access at the same
time, this is one of the main reasons why always on broadband was
invented.

Why do you have to do radius etcetera if you trust the physical lines
your customers are coming in on?  Let's look at Opus interactive
for a moment, what comes out of the modems of their St. Helens customers
that constitutes authentication information?  They connect to an ATM
network, but does that network require any authentication or is it
statically set up?  I remember having to put two fixed numbers into my
modem for service in Scappoose.  Seems to me that you can offer dhcp to
the right person by putting a custom configured instance on the correct
physical segment.  The only problem I see is that the ATM network is
probably run through a dumb concentrator/bridge causing all the
information about which segment traffic is coming from to be lost.

Right now I am going through servers on static ip addresses connected to
bridged DSL.  I guess Opus just plain trusts us.  There is no dhcp to
get onto the subnet.  I wonder if I'm still the only bridged DSL
customer in Scappoose or if others have gone this route?  

I have a hard time with the notion that you can't have all of your
DSL or cable customers on static subnets because of profit margins.
I also have a hard time with the notion that ISP's even necessarily 
use authentication.  I wish Opus would require authentication for
it's smtp service and I wish it would offer encrypted imap sessions.

From ianburrell at gmail.com  Tue Jan  2 16:35:28 2007
From: ianburrell at gmail.com (Ian Burrell)
Date: Tue, 2 Jan 2007 16:35:28 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Phone/PDA Recommendations
In-Reply-To: <1167433151.25615.93.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
References: <B0028771870@bct-mail.bctonline.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0612290905490.7832@blackbox.fnordora.org>
	<1167416238.6848.7.camel@willow.odshp.com>
	<1167433151.25615.93.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <d91f09cd0701021635j54f46f6fo159d40f5a2ec16fd@mail.gmail.com>

On 12/29/06, someone <plug_0 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
> Why combine a cell phone and a palmtop?  As far as video
> telephony goes, most people don't want to pay for broadband
> on a portable phone.  Phones are pretty simple devices, why
> complicate that?  OSS software seems to have been slow to
> penetrate the palmtop/PDA market.  Are there any efforts
> to design a palmtop or PDA specifically for Linux users?
>

It is nice to carry a single device.  I used to carry a phone for
making calls and a PDA for personal organization.  My Treo does both
functions and is the same size as the PDA.  The mobile networking
makes it even better than both.  The qwerty keyboard is handy for
sending short emails and text messages.  The camera is crappy but it
is useful when you need it.  With an SD card, it can play music. It
can't be used for the general web sites but it can be used for
checking the weather, finding Trimet schedules, or reading Wikipedia.
The screen is small but I have read ebooks on it because it was
available.

> A PDA that is wifi and Linux ready makes a lot of sense.  I don't
> understand all the hype about bluetooth.  My impression is that
> it is a hobbled wireless technology.  IR is badly hobbled these
> days also.
>

Wifi is nice because when it is available it is usally free.  The
advantage of the mobile data plans is that they are available in more
places.  The 3G ones are even comparable in speed.  A smartphone
already has them available.  There are a few smartphones which have
both wifi and mobile data.  My impression is that the carriers prefer
smartphones without wifi because they want the money from expensive
data plans.

> IR was hobbled supposedly because students were cheating on tests
> by communicating with someone remotely using the IR feature of their
> calculators.  I have no idea why you can't change the design of
> classrooms to deal with IR abuse or simply flunk cheaters instead
> of ruining a superb technology.
>

Bluetooth is a much better replacement for IR than a competitor to
Wifi.  It is designed for exchanging data between mobile devices.
Compared to IR, it is omnidirectional, faster, and longer range.
Compared of Wifi, it is slower, shorter range, cheaper, and lower
power.  Bluetooth has specialized protocols like USB. I use it to sync
between my laptop and Treo; send contacts and files to other phones;
and use it as modem for my laptop.  Other people use Bluetooth
headsets or headphones.

> With palmtops and PDAs, you have a small screen to work on and usually
> no keyboard.  These problems aren't easily solved unless someone
> perfects holographic projection.  You can't make the picture
> bigger by attaching a huge LCD monitor.  Does it make sense to pay
> for and have the equivalent of a high end computer in a palm top
> form factor?  I don't think it does.  With the restrictions, it makes
> more sense for the machine to be a personal organizer and scientific
> calculator than a full blown computer.  Show me a $100 palm top that
> can: be used to connect to evolution and ical on a full sized computer,
> be used as a scientific calculator, be used to scribble notes on, and be
> used to keep contact information handy.  And oh yeah, I want a credit
> card slot on the side of it so I can keep track of my credit and debit
> card balances by simply swiping them when they are used and entering
> the amount that has been withdrawn or deposited.  It would be nice to
> tweak checkbook tracker for this.
>

The tiny screen and keyboard aren't a replacement for a laptop or
desktop computer. It is handy because it is always with me.  It can
access the network from anywhere in civilization.  My Treo is a
organizer and scientific calculator. The advantage of Palm is that
lots of people have written software for it which makes it a
general-purpose computer.

 - Ian

From raanders at acm.org  Wed Jan  3 11:01:42 2007
From: raanders at acm.org (Roderick A. Anderson)

Subject: [PLUG] NFS/CIF Annotation ( dia? )
Message-ID: <459BFD96.5020608@acm.org>

Is there something similar to ER diagram notation for NFS and CIF 
shares/mounts?

We have 20+ physical systems ( Linux, MAC, and Windows ) that share and 
mount drives.  Several of the physical systems are Linux-Vservers so the 
mounts are then _sub_ mounted -- from the host to the guests.  And some 
of the systems both share and mount.

I'd like to build a diagram of all these.

Is there any (semi-)standard way to diagram the shared resources and 
where they are mount?

Searching 'nfs|cif diagrams|diagramming' etc. gives no joy.


TIA,
Rod
--

On Wed, 3 Jan 2007, Roderick A. Anderson wrote:

> Is there something similar to ER diagram notation for NFS and CIF 
> shares/mounts?

Rod,

   Have you looked at building a network graph? I believe that Graphviz will
do the job for you. Each system can be a node, and the shared filesystems
and directories can be directed edges.

   While the results may look like a plate of spaghetti, with different
colors and line attributes it will all make sense. Perhaps.

On 1/3/07, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
>
>    For some reason, the sound went away on my main system. There is no
> /proc/asound/. I reinstalled the alsa drivers, ran 'modprobe -a ac97'

> cannot get any results.

>    What do I do to load the modules so that /proc/asound/ appears? That's

> first thing I need to fix.


There are a bunch of modules that get loaded for sound.

Run /sbin/lsmod and see what comes up.

On my system at home I get:

 [alan at blackbox ~]$ /sbin/lsmod
Module                  Size  Used by
ipv6                  308321  28
parport_pc             33837  1
lp                     16897  0
parport                47949  2 parport_pc,lp
autofs4                27849  1
rfcomm                 47713  0
l2cap                  29121  5 rfcomm
bluetooth              60613  4 rfcomm,l2cap
sunrpc                198777  1
video                  22473  0
button                  9185  0
battery                12617  0
acpi_memhotplug         6976  0
ac                      6857  0
8139cp                 28481  0
uhci_hcd               29273  0
ehci_hcd               39501  0
i2c_viapro             10969  0
i2c_core               28353  1 i2c_viapro
snd_via82xx            35177  0
gameport               20433  1 snd_via82xx
snd_ac97_codec        123261  1 snd_via82xx
snd_ac97_bus            3777  1 snd_ac97_codec
snd_seq_dummy           5189  0
snd_seq_oss            42405  0
snd_seq_midi_event     10305  1 snd_seq_oss
snd_seq                71257  5 snd_seq_dummy,snd_seq_oss,snd_seq_midi_event
snd_pcm_oss            50769  0
snd_mixer_oss          21185  1 snd_pcm_oss
snd_pcm               111049  3 snd_via82xx,snd_ac97_codec,snd_pcm_oss
snd_timer              31305  2 snd_seq,snd_pcm
snd_page_alloc         12497  2 snd_via82xx,snd_pcm
snd_mpu401_uart        11329  1 snd_via82xx
snd_rawmidi            33217  1 snd_mpu401_uart
snd_seq_device         11729  4
snd_seq_dummy,snd_seq_oss,snd_seq,snd_rawmidi
snd                    76001  11
snd_via82xx,snd_ac97_codec,snd_seq_oss,snd_seq,snd_pcm_oss,snd_mixer_oss,snd_pcm,snd_timer,snd_mpu401_uart,snd_rawmidi,snd_seq_device
soundcore              13153  1 snd
8139too                33089  0
mii                     7361  2 8139cp,8139too
floppy                 74009  0
dm_snapshot            19985  0
dm_zero                 3009  0
dm_mirror              26025  0
ext3                  148561  2
jbd                    68329  1 ext3
dm_mod                 71945  6 dm_snapshot,dm_zero,dm_mirror


Of these, the "sndcore" module seems to load in the "snd" module which loads
in everything else.  You may have only part of the sound modules loaded.
ac97 is a codec, not a specific chip.

Check /etc/modprobe.conf to see what the system believes you need for
sound.  Here are the lines from mine:

alias snd-card-0 snd-via82xx
options snd-card-0 index=0
options snd-via82xx index=0
remove snd-via82xx { /usr/sbin/alsactl store 0 >/dev/null 2>&1 || : ; };
/sbin/modprobe -r --ignore-remove snd-via82xx

Hope that helps.

> shares/mounts?
> 
> We have 20+ physical systems ( Linux, MAC, and Windows ) that share and 
> mount drives.  Several of the physical systems are Linux-Vservers so the 
> mounts are then _sub_ mounted -- from the host to the guests.  And some 
> of the systems both share and mount.

You realize if your NFS/CIFS exports/mounts are that complex, you are
*asking* (demanding really) for trouble?

> I'd like to build a diagram of all these.
> 
> Is there any (semi-)standard way to diagram the shared resources and 
> where they are mount?
> 
> Searching 'nfs|cif diagrams|diagramming' etc. gives no joy.

Perhaps because no one else was insane enough to build a cluster like
that?

xmlArray['project']['newsgroups']['newsgroup attr']['name']

From ryan.mannon at gmail.com  Thu Jan  4 00:14:33 2007
From: ryan.mannon at gmail.com (Ryan)
Date: Thu, 4 Jan 2007 00:14:33 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Remotely disable Internet access with a cell phone?
Message-ID: <a4ba175e0701040014o505465fbsd8326554567b367f@mail.gmail.com>

Here is a little idea that I have been mulling around, I think it might work
and I would just like to get some input from some guru's.

My sister in law has two teenagers at home. The boy 14, doesn't spend too
much time on the net other than e-mail and myspace. Now the 13yr old girl is
a fiend. Every spare moment she has she is on the net chatting, downloading,
myspacing, staying up all night in front of the screen, etc, etc. I have
suggested many a time to remove the cable modem when my sister in law is not
at home or when she goes to bed. But she is lazy when it comes to policing
what her kids are doing on the computer. Thats a whole different issue that
I personally label as not interested in knowing what her kids are doing on
line. Makes me mad but they are not my kids. Anyway back to the "idea".

Here is a possible situation: Mom grounds kid from computer. Mom goes to
work and kids are still home. Kids turn on the computer and are surfing all
day long. Kids turn off computer before mom gets home. But what if mom was
able to dial a number on her cell phone and a voice says "press one to
disable network access, press two to enable network access". Network access
is unavailable until mom dials number and re-enables network access no
matter what account on the computer is logged on.

I have a feeling that this would have to be enabled in the modem, but what
if the program was installed on the computer and a very specific key
combination was the only way to open the program? I've been doing some
research on Google but the closest I can come are a few programs that block
access for a specific amount of time.

Has anybody heard of something like this, is it feasible? Anybody who has
any ideas on how or if, I would love to hear from you.

Thanks in advance

-ryan

Assuming this is a Windows box, just configure it for a static IP and
give the kids limited user accounts. An attempt to directly connect the
computer to any modem would fail, and the router could be configured to
log the event (either when it boots up or when it has to renew the dhcp
lease when reconnected to the modem)

Message-ID: <459D1E25.9020803@cesmail.net>

I've got a Gentoo Linux system, and I just noticed that Thunderbird 
appears to be using the processor to some extent continuously -- if you 
run "top", Thunderbird sits at the top of the list much more than I'd 
expect when it's sitting idle and minimized. Being a performance 
engineer, running "top" is somewhat instinctive, so I'm of the opinion 
that this is something that came in when the latest release (version 2 
beta 1) got installed. Has anyone else seen this?

Just to be clear, the technology I asked about, and some have commented
on, is not the same as broadband-over-powerlines.  The names are a bit
too close, and perhaps the home networking product uses some of the
same tricks.  But I haven't seen anything to suggest that it interfers
with RF transmissions.

I assume she could dial into a modem attached to a computer on the LAN
and enable/disable interfaces that way, but what's to prevent the
daughter from unplugging the modem cable?  (I don't know what software
to put on the modem; I have a winmodem so I've never played with it.)

Personally, I handle this sort of thing by looking at how much traffic
has been moved over the router at different times.  If my daughter's
looking sleepy and it turns out somebody downloaded 50M between 2 and
4 am the previous night there's hell to pay.

> > aren't any perk's to having static IP's, but from a management side
> > there are a lot of perk's to DHCP as well.
> 
> Why is dynamically assigning ip addresses better than staticly
> assigning them?  What is the advantage of putting customers on
> random global ip addresses within a single subnet, or less than
> that?  As far as anonymizing people is concerned, having a
> different ip address every time you browse the Internet isn't
> much protection.  Think about it, if someone wants to harass
> you and you are 1 out of say 255 ip addresses, that isn't very
> many possibilities if they can spy on all them.  Then once they
> notice the habits of one of those ip addresses matches yours,
> they might be able to port scan the ip address and fire off an
> attack.  How often do dynamic ip addresses change?  With Comcast,
> you can easily go for a few months with the same number.
> 

The main practical reason for automatically assigning IP addresses from a
pool is to serve mobile users.  Think of WiFi connects in a coffee shop for
instance.  Or corporate users taking their laptops when traveling to another
office location.

Charles L. Sliger,  Information Systems Engineer,  chaz at bctonline.com
"No matter where you go, there you are..."

> and give the kids limited user accounts. An attempt to directly 
> connect the computer to any modem would fail, and the router could 
> be configured to log the event (either when it boots up or when it

Then one of the kids will discover Knoppix, and she will just boot 
from a LiveCD. :-(

Ultimately, the only iron-clad way to prevent Internet access would be 
for Mom to physically disconnect the DSL/cable modem and ensure it 
stays unavailable (hidden, out of the house, or ...).

If tamper-evident is all the Mom needs, then, yeah, putting a decent 
password on the router and keeping an eye on the logs will do fine.

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Tue Jan  2 13:03:41 2007
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (someone)
Date: Tue, 02 Jan 2007 13:03:41 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Study OS landscape...
Message-ID: <1167771822.27756.55.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>

I have been trying to study what operating systems exist in the current
landscape and why.  I'm going to report briefly on what I found on
http://www.freeos.com/ and through google in general.

What I have for a list so far:

  *  SkyOS

     Syllable

     BeOS

     ReactOS
 
     Linux

     FreeBSD

     Freedos

     OS/2

     Solaris and other proprietary Unices.

     There are a lot of Microsoft OS'es...

     Novell Netware

There are some obscure ones as well:

     TOPS-10

     MIPS

     AmigaOS

     PDP-11

     ...

Don't know what SkyOS is all about, but I guess it is proprietary.
I tried to google to a web site for it, 
http://www.skyos.com/ wouldn't open up.

I do not understand what is driving Syllable.  Supposedly it is a
post AtheOS system, whatever AtheOS is.  I don't like the idea that
you need a Pentium to run Syllable nor do I understand why such a
big deal is made about integrating the GUI into the kernel.  The
blurb for Syllable is that it is meant to be an OS that is easy
to use in a home/office environment.  It is released under the 
GPL.  I ran into some X bashing.  Syllable runs on the AFS 
filesystem and apparently has problems with grub.

I know there is a BeOS bible, but I don't know much more than that
about this particular OS.

ReactOS is falling behind, 0.3.1 isn't out yet.  At least the web
site is back up.  This seems to be the only operating system at 
this time that is being designed to be a drop in Windows XP 
replacement.

Linux is being raked on freeos.com, a person who says he is 59 but not
a computer guru complains that while Linux doesn't leave him worrying
about viruses, it doesn't let him run much software either.  The
reviewer hates Windows 95 but he says that XP Pro right now is a 
better fit for him than Ubuntu.

I am impressed with gem in Freedos 1.0.  Anyone ever heard of or tried
seal?  Freedos is an MSDOS replacement that is apparently promoted by
Jim Hall.  The main reason for freedos apparently is that a number of
embedded software vendors still need a dos system.

OS/2 is on the list because I know it exists, but I don't know it's
status or anything else about it really at this point.

I am not an expert on Solaris et. al, but I have heard that Solaris is
good for music software.  I have also run into readme compiling software
under Linux which say that Solaris has brain dead compilers.

A screenshot of a Vista beta showed a 3D look.  Excuse me Microsoft, but
I don't want to waste the resources of my Cadillac on fancy windows.
The rumor that Vista is positioned to be anti vmware licensed has me
wondering why the ReactOS team is even bothering to create a build
environment for it.  Microsoft isn't cutting edge when it comes to
leaving the beta stage if Vista is behind schedule.  Rumor is that
Microsoft's SourceSafe isn't all that great and that it can take
months for something to get fixed right.  Claims that Microsoft is
too big and that IBM once was also seem to be swirling around the
Internet lately.

Netware is an interesting beast.  I don't know what the state of it is
today.  I didn't like a class I tried to take on Netware 4.0 at PCC.  
Netware seems to be an OS that doesn't offer a client, I guess the
client is supposed to be either SUSE Linux or Windows.

What irritated me is that a lot of web sites seem to be running a
Microsoft SQL Server ad.  I'm trying to look beyond Microsoft and
Linux and am sure enough getting bombarded with Microsoft SQL ads.
I think trains and ships have nothing to do with database
software per se, so I don't get the ads.  Is Microsoft trying to
say that Vista is so fat that you'll need a whole skyscraper or
a large ship to house the server that runs MicrosoftSQL server?

As far as a comment that Linux needs a universal installer across
all distributions to compete effectively with Windows, I dunno about
that.



Some interesting questions going forward:
-----------------------------------------

How can Linux support hardware better in the future and get away from
people needing to do manual configuration? ***

Are any of the non Microsoft and non Linux/FreeBSD operating systems
which don't pursue compatibility with a Microsoft product going to
flower?

Are obscure hobbyist operating systems worth any consideration?  Do
they make any sense?

If 32 bit Freedos becomes a viable option, what hardware will it 
require and how far will it go?  

Microsoft set out on a quest to take the command line away and 
eradicate dos in 1995, was it the right choice?  

Is the future going to be about 5 operating systems or many more 
than that?  ***

How are issues surrounding access to hardware specifications for 
proper driver development looking: 1, 5, 10 years into the 
future? ***

A system I didn't list, Minix 3, was that an inappropriate omission?

How many operating systems from today's operating system landscape are
missing from my list?

Should X be the only GUI platform for Linux?

> > >From a managment standpoint DHCP is much easier but requires more
> upfront

> you
> > risk having only roughly 63 customers per /24 subnet (depending on how
> its
> > engineered) so you're looking at additional cost as you grow because
> you're
> > paying someone to submit applications to ARIN for new subnets as well as
> > paying the registration fee's. Of course if you go DHCP then you have to
> > deal with things like the MPAA and RIAA complaints and being able to
> > accurately correlate authentication and IP data to a specific account
> > which takes time and money :-)
> >
> > This idea could be argued for ever and ever but I guess comes down to
> the
> > time and money and engineering you want to put into a network
>
> Well, DSL service providers offer static subnets where they don't do
> DHCP for those customers.  With the advent of IPV6, ip addresses should
> get cheaper.  As far as arguing that you can only put 255 people on
> a /24 and that this isn't profitable, we are talking about DSL customers
> or cable customers that are always supposed to have access.  If you only
> have 1 subnet of 255 hosts and more than that many customers, say three
> times as many, probably only a third of your customer can use the
> service simultaneously.  Considering people tend
> to want access at the same time, 66% on average are probably going to be
> angry when they get blocked.  There used to be dial-up ISP's that hit
> 13:1 ratios for people trying to get on.  People don't expect a
> broadband experience to be like a dial-up one.  Besides that, there
> probably aren't any dial-up ISP's that are that bad anymore.


I was referring to the fact that if you are using routed versus bridged
subnets on a /24 that you'd be looking at a total of 63 static customers
which his a waste of IP space IMHO unless they are paying a premium for the
service. Its entirely feasible to do bridged /24 static subnets as well.
There shouldn't ever be contention on a DHCP broadband network. You can
assign /23 or even larger if your customer base in that large.



The average DSL customer pays a minimum of $25/month.  That is $1575
> gross from 255 paying customers each month.  Now what does the OC-3
> feeding these people cost?  How much is lost to taxes?  $50+/month is
> common for bridged customers with a subnet that have decent bandwidth.
> The fees to ARIN, not an issue for ISP's because they already bridge
> customers without doing dhcp and charge the customer for it in the
> process.
>
> Dynamic assignment of ip addresses only means you can serve more
> customers if you are lucky enough to find people that are willing to
> time share.  Considering that most people want Net access at the same
> time, this is one of the main reasons why always on broadband was
> invented.
>
> Why do you have to do radius etcetera if you trust the physical lines

> physical segment.  The only problem I see is that the ATM network is
> probably run through a dumb concentrator/bridge causing all the
> information about which segment traffic is coming from to be lost.


You can do it without authentication. The company I work for has a PPPoE
product (which I detest but have to support) so I had authentication on the
brain when I wrote the earlier email. I think authenticated user sessions
are better IMHO because you have documentation on usage as well as
information you might need in the case of a MPAA or RIAA notice if the
company were to take them seriously.

There is such a thing as being too terse; an lsmod would've been useful.

would have been even more useful.  It'd also have been nice to know what

AC97 is an interface protocol between the chipset and the codec; its
driver is loaded automatically by the chipset driver, e.g. snd-intel8x0.

Don't forget: http://catb.org/~esr/faqs/smart-questions.html

Well ... I'm not going to answer *all* of your questions, but if you're 
interested in operating systems that are in moderate to widespread 
*current* use, there are a lot on your list that shouldn't be there. 
Here's the list off the top of my head:

1. Windows -- I'm going to lump all of these together, even though there 
are two Windows lineages, the NT/2000/XP/Vista lineage and the 
95/98/98SE lineage. And I'm going to include DOS in here, as well as 
FreeDOS. Basically anything Microsoft or a clone thereof.

2. UNIX-ish -- this is a broad category, but I think the major living 
dialects are Tru64, HPUX, AIX, BSD, Linux, Solaris, IRIX  and SCO. You 
occasionally find such things as Motorola 88000s running SVR3 and SVR4, 
but they're rate.

3. MacOS X -- technically speaking, this should be a UNIX-ish, but it's 
unique enough that it deserves its own slot. Besides, I need a place to 
put the previous versions of Mac OS.

4. IBM Mainframes: VM/CMS, VM/MVS, regular old MVS and zSeries VM/Linux. 
I don't know what the AS/400 OS is called; whatever it is, put it here. :)

5. OpenVMS -- I think there are still a fair number of Alphas running 
this, and maybe even some VAXes running its predecessor.

6. Really obscure -- I actually know people who still run OS/2 and CP-M, 
and there are probably folks running Unisys or Honeywell mainframes too.

7. Real-time operating systems -- it's been years since I knew any of 
these names, but they still exist. You can find variants of Linux that 
will meet hard real-time requirements, but I'm pretty sure others exist. 
In fact, there are a couple of projects that run Linux as a layer on top 
of a hard-real-time microkernel.

That's my list, strictly from memory.

-- 
M. Edward (Ed) Borasky, FBG, AB, PTA, PGS, MS, MNLP, NST, ACMC(P)
http://borasky-research.blogspot.com/

From m0gely at telestream.com  Thu Jan  4 20:45:18 2007
From: m0gely at telestream.com (m0gely)
Date: Thu, 04 Jan 2007 20:45:18 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] E-mail server?
In-Reply-To: <B0029078117@bct-mail.bctonline.com>
References: <B0029078117@bct-mail.bctonline.com>
Message-ID: <459DD7DE.4060905@telestream.com>

Charles Sliger wrote:

> The main practical reason for automatically assigning IP addresses from a
> pool is to serve mobile users.  Think of WiFi connects in a coffee shop for
> instance.  Or corporate users taking their laptops when traveling to another
> office location.

DHCP offers many benefits period.  While mobile users and your coffee 
shop are two examples, they aren't the "main" reason.  It's a management 
style that each network admin will have to decide on their own. 
Personally I use a mixed environment on most networks I set up.  All 
servers, printers and appliances get static IP's which work well for 
managing, and the client machines all get dynamic IP's.  It's easy 
enough to start the DCHP pool at something like .50 rather than .2, so 
you can use .1 through .49 for static assignments.

-- 
- m0gely

I think at this point having always-on internet access at all might be

configured to manually enter authentication (ala dial-up), then keep the 
password a well kept secret.  Disconnecting and hiding the modem I 
suppose is an option, but entering a user name and password is much more 
convenient.

From jeme at brelin.net  Fri Jan  5 03:18:29 2007
From: jeme at brelin.net (Jeme A Brelin)
Date: Fri, 5 Jan 2007 03:18:29 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Resolution needs resolution (Was: LCD thingies)
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0611302335320.2617@shaft.bitmine.net>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0611302335320.2617@shaft.bitmine.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0701050303310.2617@shaft.bitmine.net>


So I bought one.  I got this fancy Samsung SyncMaster 215TW (and had my 
first wholly positive Fry's experience -- in stock and lower than 
advertised price.  Plus they let me plug it in and check it out in the 
store).

When I plugged it in, nothing happened.  But I think that was some sensing 
thing in my monitor when moving from the VGA to the DVI connector. 
Rebooted.  No big.

Immediate problem:  Console is in bad shape.  Looks really pretty, but 
only displays the top 2/3 of the screen.  It uses the full width, but 
thinks it's more square.  So can I change my console to 80x18 instead of 
80x24?  Or something?  It sure does look nice, though.

OK, so I let it boot without doing anything to xorg.conf.  It came up with 
a very pretty 1280x1024 box in the middle of the screen.  I log in and can 
use this lovely (did I mention how good it looks?) display.  But it's 
1280x1024 centered in a sea of 1680x1050 goodness.  How can I fill the 
whole space?

Well, first I just kind of see if X can handle it.  I ran dpkg-reconfigure 
xserver-xorg and selected 1680x1050 as the default resolution and 
restarted X.  What I got was ugly.  A strip in the middle of the screen 
maybe 600 pixels wide and what looked like my display manager login window 
peeking out of the corner on the far right of this region.  The rest was 
black.  And what was there was kind of stripy and not at all the normal 
order of things.

I played around with a few modelines and the horzontal sync and vertical 
refresh and got basically exactly the same thing every retry.

I looked into the html manual that came with this thing and found the 
following seemingly helpful data:

<quote src="manual">

Preset Timing Modes
If the signal transferred from the computer is the same as the following 
Preset Timing Modes, the screen will be adjusted automatically. However, 
if the signal differs, the screen may go blank while the power LED is on. 
Refer to the video card manual and adjust the screen as follows.

[bunch of modes deleted - J.]

Display Mode:		VESA 1680x1050
Horizontal Freq:	65.290
Vertical Freq:		59.954
Pixel Clock:		146.250
Sync Polarity (H/V):	-/+

</quote>

So that seems like something quite useful, eh?  None of the modelines I 
could generate gave me exactly that pixel clock.

Oh, and here's some interesting stuff from the failed xorg.0.log:

<quote src="xorg.0.log">
(II) NV(0): Probing for EDID on I2C bus A...
(II) NV(0): I2C device "DDC:ddc2" registered at address 0xA0.
(II) NV(0): I2C device "DDC:ddc2" removed.
(II) NV(0):   ... none found
(--) NV(0): HW is currently programmed for DFP
(II) NV(0): Using DFP on CRTC 0
(--) NV(0): Panel size is 1680 x 1050
(--) NV(0): VideoRAM: 65536 kBytes
(==) NV(0): Using gamma correction (1.0, 1.0, 1.0)
</quote>

It then goes on to tell me information that leads me to believe clock and 
sync information is not being sent from the monitor.

Anybody have any nice suggestions so that I can use this beautiful, 
beautiful thing?

J.
-- 
    -----------------
      Jeme A Brelin
     jeme at brelin.net

> over in my chair when the test sound came out of my laptop at a rather

Tony,

   Well, I managed to read one reply to my post on comp.os.linux.slackware
before the Aracnet news server trashed the entire thread. Well, I was able
to sustain the connections long enough to read a couple more replies.

   It turns out that ALSA is not included in the 2.4 kernels; that uses the
soon-to-be-deprecated OSS. But, Patrick complies and prepares ALSA packages
for the default kernel in the distribution. So, I have two viable options
(and one easily ignored):

   1) Build ALSA myself: easy to do since the source is on the distribution
disks (and the web repositories) and each includes a Slackbuild file to
build the package for me.

   2) Use a 2.6 kernel. Which I'll try on the portables first -- this
weekend. I will upgrade my server/workstation, too, to Slackware-11.0 and
can give the 2.6.18.6 kernel a try. I really don't care what kernel I run as
long as it handles my simple needs.

   And the other choice is to use OSS.

   Whew! I had no idea this was the case, despite not seeing the required
drivers in 2.4.32 or 2.4.33.4.

   My thanks to you and Alan.

Rich

working with vesa and scan the information in the X log  Then you will 
hopefully have enough info to make nv work.

Alan Olsen wrote:

>> I nearly fell over in my chair when the test sound came out of my
>> laptop at a rather loud volume.
> 
> Never try this on a bus.
> 
> Been there. Done that. Scared the crap out of everyone.

I've had a similar problem on occasion. On one laptop, I have external
speakers plugged in, and so I can just turn the speaker volume control down.

It occurred to me that having a plug to insert into the headphone jack
would let me get the machine booted and the software controlled volume
turned down before letting the signal get to the internal speakers. I
assume that it would be a good idea to have a couple of resistors (8
ohm?) wired into that plug to present a speaker-like load to the
laptop's sound hardware, but I don't know if that's really necessary. It
would be one more thing to have to carry around, but it could prevent
unpleasantness on the bus.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Fri Jan  5 11:47:10 2007
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Fri, 05 Jan 2007 11:47:10 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Resolution needs resolution (Was: LCD thingies)
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0701050303310.2617@shaft.bitmine.net>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0611302335320.2617@shaft.bitmine.net>
	<Pine.LNX.4.61.0701050303310.2617@shaft.bitmine.net>
Message-ID: <1168026430.7842.10.camel@willow.odshp.com>

On Fri, 2007-01-05 at 03:18 -0800, Jeme A Brelin wrote:

> Well, first I just kind of see if X can handle it.  I ran dpkg-reconfigure 
> xserver-xorg and selected 1680x1050 as the default resolution and 
> restarted X.  What I got was ugly.  A strip in the middle of the screen 
> maybe 600 pixels wide and what looked like my display manager login window 
> peeking out of the corner on the far right of this region.  The rest was 
> black.  And what was there was kind of stripy and not at all the normal 
> order of things.

I found when installed mine (the 205BW), after configuring the video
card settings I was still getting something like the above.  Sometimes I
had a clear 1280x1024 with discolored bounding box on the bottom and
right; sometimes it used the whole screen but was distorted in other
ways (including the mouse input being shifted by about an inch from the
displayed cursor).  I found that I had to use the (GNOME or Fedora, not
sure which) "Screen Resolution" tool, which I think manipulates the
XRANDR setting.  Curiously, the login splash screen *wasn't* corrupted.
Once I set this to the appropriate resolution, the display was correct;
it seems it was changing the resolution via XRANDR when the session
started.  You might examine the XRANDR settings and see if there's
something in the session changing them.

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
http://nakedape.cc

Hi! This is closer to a hardware question than a Linux question, but I
hope you can help me out anyway.

I would like to record line level audio from a stereo amplifier on my
Thinkpad T42. There are only two 1/8" audio jacks on the machine:
headphone and microphone.

The chip is an "Analog Devices AD1981B". The documentation for this chip
states that the allowed input voltage levels for the Mic input are the
same as for the Line input (not accessible on this machine), provided
that the "+20dB Mic Boost" is turned off. But when I try to record
line-level input through the microphone input, the apparent levels are
too high and appear only in one channel, even if the "Stereo Mic" switch
is turned on.

This same signal records just fine through the line input on a desktop
equipped with an emu10k1 card.

Is it possible to record stereo line-level input on my Thinkpad? If so,
how do I do it?

Thanks for any advice you can offer!

   For the record, here's the almost complete story.

   On my notebook (Slackware-11.0), the 2.6.16.36 kernel did not have the
requisite sound card driver. So, I built and installed a 2.6.18.6 kernel and
rebooted.

   Now alsamixer works, and the intel8x0 driver is loaded for the Intel card
(which is found and listed in /proc/asound/cards).

   I can play a music mp3 using xmms, but there's no volume control. Xmms
complains that the oss_set_volume() failed to open mixer device /dev/mixer.
But, there is a /dev/mixer as a soft link to /dev/sound, and they have perms
of 777. This is one issue to be resolved.

   However, I still have no sound with the videos on reuters.com. I've no
idea why, but this is the second issue to be resolved.

   Much closer. This weekend I'll upgrade the server/workstation and the
other portable to Slackware-11.0 and the 2.6.18.6 kernels and start working
my way through issues left on them.

Rich

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Fri Jan  5 20:14:46 2007
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sat, 6 Jan 2007 04:14:46 +0000
Subject: [PLUG] IEEE Speaker: SMP and Embedded Real-Time Myths
Message-ID: <20070106041446.GA31943@gate.kl-ic.com>


Linux and multiprocessing.

FYI - Keith 

----- Forwarded message from Pradeep Kumar <pradeep at ieee.org> -----
From: "Pradeep Kumar" <pradeep at ieee.org>
To: comp_coms at ieee-or.org
Subject: IEEE Speaker: SMP and Embedded Real-Time Myths

Institute of Electrical and Electronic Engineers ( IEEE)'s Oregon & SW
Washington
Communications & Computer Society chapters present

*IEEE Seminar: SMP and Embedded Real-Time Myths*


*Speaker: Paul E. McKenney, IBM*

When: Thursday, January 18th 2007;

Time: 6:00 PM ~ 8:30 PM

Cost: Free and open to the public (registration required)

Place: Oregon Graduate Institute of Science and Technology (OGI), Wilson
Clark Center, 20000 NW Walker Rd, Beaverton, OR 97006

To register go to: http://www.cpd.ogi.edu/coursespecific.asp?pam=2184



*Abstract*

With the advent of multi-threaded/multi-core CPUs, even embedded realtime
applications are starting to run on SMP systems--for example, both the Xbox
360 and PS3 are multi-threaded, and there have even been SMP ARM processors!
As this trend continues, there will be an increasing need for realtime
response from SMP systems. Because not all embedded systems vendors will be
willing or able to create or purchase SMP realtime operating systems, we can
expect that a number of them will make use of Linux.

Because of this change, a number of real-time tenets have now become myths.
This talk exposes these myths, and then discusses some of the challenges
that Linux is surmounting in order to meet the needs of this new
SMP-real-time-embedded world.*Speaker Bio*

*Bio*

Paul E. McKenney is a distinguished engineer at IBM and has worked on SMP,
NUMA, and RCU algorithms for longer than he cares to admit (see
www.rdrop.com/users/paulmck if you don't believe this). He has recently
become quite interested in getting real-time response from mid-range SMP
systems. Prior to that, he worked on packet-radio and Internet protocols
(but long before the Internet became popular), system administration,
real-time systems, and even business applications. His hobbies include
running and family activities.

----- End forwarded message -----

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993

   This is a minor issue, but one I'd like to understand and fix (if
possible). It involves the mail log summary about which I've written in the

miss a day, but either both run or neither runs.

# run mail log summary just after midnight each day.
2 0 * * * /etc/cron.daily/1pflogsumm

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sat Jan  6 10:16:48 2007
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sat, 6 Jan 2007 18:16:48 +0000
Subject: [PLUG] new printer
Message-ID: <20070106181648.GA8954@gate.kl-ic.com>


More fun with the power meter.   A few years ago, I picked up an old
Tektronix (now Xerox) Phaser 350 - a color wax printer - very cheaply. 
I don't print color very often, but it served for the times that I do. 

Then I measured its power use.  In spite of Tektronix's misleading ads,
the printer is NOT eco-friendly.  From a cold start, it needs about 10
minutes to warm up the wax ink before it can print, and in the process 
wastes a few CCs of it.  The alternative is to leave it powered up with
standby disabled, but that means it is consuming about 250 watts, or
about $180 per year of electricity.  

I hate inkjets;  they dry up and clog, and the supplies are very
expensive (compute the dollars per liter sometime - astonishing!).
The printed pages run if they get wet, and can stain other things.

So ... yesterday I bought an HP2605DN from Staples for $450.  Again,
the supplies are very expensive, but cheaper than inkjet and they
don't dry up.  The printer is reasonably fast, Postscript 3, 100BT
ethernet, has a duplexer, and a straight-through paper path so it
will be harder to jam.  There is a CUPS ppd file available.  The
printer consumes about 300 watts while actually printing (it looks
like an "instant on fuser") and 15 watts idle.  The toner cartridges
are "chipped" - they estimate toner consumption from the page count
and stop working even if refilled - but that seems to be true of
almost everything these days.  It was pretty quick to install, even
without specific Linux instructions on the CD, and including manual
reading for CUPs.  It has a web page interface built into the machine.
I spent more time at Staples trying to get someone to bring some paper
for testing it than I spent on install time back home (hint - bring your
own test materials, including card and label stock).  For now, it seems
to fill the need, but only time will tell if it is a good investment.

The most interesting alternative in the price range is the Xerox 6120N,
which is $300 on sale from Staples but only available for delivery.
It has more memory and allegedly a better image.  On the minus side,
it is a four-pass rather than a one pass machine, making it very
slow;  it consumes more power, and it has a lot more items to replace
in maintenance.  I did some calculations, and the extra wait time at
my hourly rate would exceed the price difference long before I use up
one set of toner cartridges.  Still, if anyone has one of these I
would be interested in their experiences.

I spent way too much time researching before the purchase, and many
machines dropped off the list - no postscript, no local supply of
toner, no gray-market refills, too slow, too expensive for my needs. 
Again, I am interested in the experiences of others.

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993

Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

A couple of months ago, I mentioned that it would be nice to have a
customizable search page for a web browser, so I would not have to 
use tabs or bookmarks, just pick one of the entry boxes and hit go.

It turns out that the Google customizable home page is a reasonably
close approximation to my needs.  You can select from thousands of
mostly user supplied "gadgets", and build up customized page from
them.  For example, here is my current one:

  http://www.keithl.com/googlepage.png

I have search boxes for ebay, wikipedia, linux search, imdb, and local
information for movies, calendar, and map.  In my copious spare time,
I may design gadgets for Addall, the library, and telephone search,
and perhaps a "button box" that can call up bookmarks to CUPS, my
websites, and other places I go frequently.  

This isn't perfect;  most of the gadgets take up too much space.  But
it does get me 50% of the way there.  Nice tool.

On Sat, Jan 06, 2007 at 07:18:04PM +0000, Keith Lofstrom wrote:
> I have search boxes for ebay, wikipedia, linux search, imdb, and
> local information for movies, calendar, and map.

If all you need is a simple way to query your favorite search engines,
then Firefox's "keyword" feature works great. Right-click on the text
entry box of any search form and select "Add a keyword for this
search..." Once you have the keyword search set up, all you have to do
is enter the keyword plus the search query into the browser's location
bar, and Firefox will send the query off to the appropriate URL. For
instance, you might set up a keyword search for the Internet Movie
Database such that all you have to do is control-L over to the
location bar, enter "imdb captain kangaroo", and be automatically taken to
the IMDB search results page.

-- 
Paul

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sun Jan  7 09:56:53 2007
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sun, 7 Jan 2007 17:56:53 +0000
Subject: [PLUG] CUPS and printer models
Message-ID: <20070107175652.GA30243@gate.kl-ic.com>


The following is more of a micro-HOWTO than a question or a complaint.
Hopefully, it will help somebody else (and help me remember it for
next time).  Corrections welcomed.


The CUPS printing system has a nice web page setup for new printers;
it provides the fastest way to change printer options or move a
printer to another interface or network address.  However, out of the
box it only works with a dozen types of generic printer.  What to do
if you have a printer with special features?  It may be postscript,
but what if it has multiple trays, or a duplexer, or ...?

The special features of almost all printers are described in something
called a PPD file.  Many manufacturers supply PPD files on the install
CDs - they are used by windoze and mac, too.  Also, you can go to a
number of sites to download PPDs.  I like www.linuxprinting.org .  But
where do you put your downloaded PPD, and what do you do with it?  I
have a 500 page book on CUPS, which says nothing about this.  So I
figured it out.  I think.

There are two directories containing PPD files in CUPS:
   /usr/share/cups/model/  # these are the generic models available
   /etc/cups/ppd/          # specific PPDs made for your own printers

If you modify a printer from the web GUI, it will only know about the
printers in /usr/share/cups/model/ , and will overwrite what is in 
/etc/cups/ppd/ .  So if you put your downloaded PPD in /etc/cups/ppd/,
it may get overwritten later.

So the appropriate place to put the PPD is in /usr/share/cups/model .
Typically, the PPD file you download will be an uncompressed file,
but the PPD files in these directories are compressed.  So you take
the downloaded ppd and make a gzip out of it:

  gzip  myprintermodel.ppd

... resulting in "myprintermodel.ppd.gz" in that directory.  The running
cupsd process still does not know about it though, so you have to restart
the cups daemon.  On my Fedora/Redhat machines, that is (as root):

  service cups restart

Now I can use the gui, use the modify button, and select my specific
printer model under the specific manufacturer's tab.

I don't know whether this approach will survive a distro upgrade of CUPS;
that may replace the contents of /usr/share/cups/model/.  So I save the
PPD file in my personal downloads directory.

There may be an easier way to do this, but the above works.  Now, go
out there and KILL some TREES.
  
Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs


From aaron at madebyai.com  Sun Jan  7 11:30:08 2007
From: aaron at madebyai.com (Aaron Ten Clay)
Date: Sun, 07 Jan 2007 11:30:08 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] CUPS and printer models
In-Reply-To: <20070107175652.GA30243@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <20070107175652.GA30243@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <courier.45A14A42.000039D0@gadgetrix.gadgetlife.org>

Keith Lofstrom writes: 

> 
> The following is more of a micro-HOWTO than a question or a complaint.
> Hopefully, it will help somebody else (and help me remember it for
> next time).  Corrections welcomed. 
> 
Not so much correction as possible enhancement(s) below... 

> 
> The CUPS printing system has a nice web page setup for new printers;
> it provides the fastest way to change printer options or move a
> printer to another interface or network address.

Unfortunately, some distros disable the web interface per default. I believe 
this is the trend for Debian based distros (Ubuntu/Kubuntu for sure). To 
enable the web interface on said distros, add the 'cupsys' user to the 
'shadow' group, then restart cups (see below to restart). This is done via 
the following command (as root): 

gpasswd -a cupsys shadow 


> However, out of the
> box it only works with a dozen types of generic printer_s_.  What to do
> if you have a printer with special features?  It may be postscript,
> but what if it has multiple trays, or a duplexer, or ...? 
> 
> The special features of almost all printers are described in something
> called a PPD file.  Many manufacturers supply PPD files on the install
> CDs - they are used by windoze and mac, too.  Also, you can go to a
> number of sites to download PPDs.  I like www.linuxprinting.org .  But
> where do you put your downloaded PPD, and what do you do with it?  I
> have a 500 page book on CUPS, which says nothing about this.  So I
> figured it out.  I think. 
> 
> There are two directories containing PPD files in CUPS:
>    /usr/share/cups/model/  # these are the generic models available
>    /etc/cups/ppd/          # specific PPDs made for your own printers 
> 

It is worth noting that files can be in subdirectories of the model 
directory, if so desired. 

> If you modify a printer from the web GUI, it will only know about the
> printers in /usr/share/cups/model/ , and will overwrite what is in 
> /etc/cups/ppd/ .  So if you put your downloaded PPD in /etc/cups/ppd/,
> it may get overwritten later. 
> 
> So the appropriate place to put the PPD is in /usr/share/cups/model .
> Typically, the PPD file you download will be an uncompressed file,
> but the PPD files in these directories are compressed.  So you take
> the downloaded ppd and make a gzip out of it: 
> 
>   gzip  myprintermodel.ppd 
> 

Also worth noting that ppds need not be compressed (.ppd files are also 
picked up) and it's possible to compile cups without gzip support, so 
probably best not to compress these files by default unless the existing 
ones are already compressed. 

> ... resulting in "myprintermodel.ppd.gz" in that directory.  The running
> cupsd process still does not know about it though, so you have to restart
> the cups daemon.  On my Fedora/Redhat machines, that is (as root): 
> 
>   service cups restart 
> 

I believe you can cover virtually all distros with the command: 

/etc/init.d/cups* restart 

(This gets redhat, gentoo, debian, suse, (k)ubuntu...) 

> Now I can use the gui, use the modify button, and select my specific
> printer model under the specific manufacturer's tab. 
> 
> I don't know whether this approach will survive a distro upgrade of CUPS;
> that may replace the contents of /usr/share/cups/model/.  So I save the
> PPD file in my personal downloads directory. 
> 
> There may be an easier way to do this, but the above works.  Now, go
> out there and KILL some TREES.
>   
> Keith 
> 
> --

> Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs
> _______________________________________________

> http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug

Good mini-writeup. Kudos.

Keith Lofstrom writes:

I have one, and got one for my mother because she has trouble changing 
inkjet cartridges and inkjets take _way_ too long to print a page. By my 
measurements, the printer consumes ~760W while heating, and <10W in standby 
running with 100mbps FD ethernet. While "Ready" the printer consumes ~12W 
with a 1 second 760W burst every 30 seconds, apparently all that's needed to 
keep heated. These measurements were made with a seasonic powerangel plug-in 
kilowatthour/ammeter, which may not be the most precise instrument. 

Also, I don't remember the rating in PPM but a 100 page full-color document 
takes about 3 minutes to print, and from standby to first full-color page I 
wait about 30-40 seconds. I'm no expert but I wouldn't call that "slow" by 
any standard. 

For my uses (replacing an inkjet), this printer is outstanding. It has a 
simple but effective web-interface even my mom can get around. And even 
printing from standby a single full-color sheet takes far less time than 
even the fastest inkjets, all at far less per-page cost with supplies and 
power taken into account. 

I don't know how onboard RAM effects performance, but I will say it's quite 
slow to print a full-page image at 600x600 DPI using postscript3 over 
100mbps FD ethernet. It probably takes 5 minutes before the printer even 
awakes from standby. (Possibly CUPS spooling on the server? Haven't 
diagnosed beyond observing the slowness) I haven't tried using the printer 
over USB or parallel but I can't see that being faster (though I noticed the 
USB interface is USB 2.0, not 1.0 or 1.1...) 

 --
Aaron

From randy.rowlette at gmail.com  Sun Jan  7 21:22:19 2007

From krisa at subtend.net  Mon Jan  8 16:23:33 2007
From: krisa at subtend.net (Kris)
Date: Mon, 08 Jan 2007 16:23:33 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] cisco2500
In-Reply-To: <1168026488.7842.12.camel@willow.odshp.com>
References: <727fc2ee0701050942u725e3ed5m94638b6ed6ae8c5e@mail.gmail.com>	<20070105192221.GR30613@syrinxpc.com>	<727fc2ee0701051131q5be8304dofb8e6811c3d07368@mail.gmail.com>
	<1168026488.7842.12.camel@willow.odshp.com>
Message-ID: <45A2E085.4070603@subtend.net>

Wil Cooley wrote:
> On Fri, 2007-01-05 at 11:31 -0800, User Iam wrote:
>> I will have to check..
>>
>> Now that you mention it...
>>
>> I don't think so...
> 
> The 2500 has WIC slots; I believe there are ADSL cards available for it,
> but they're ~$800, IIRC.  So, have at it.

The 2600 series has WIC slots.  2500's pre-date WIC's, and were fixed
configuration based on specific model.  Most were a mix of serial (sync
and async), 10BaseT, Token Ring, ISDN, and pre-dated xDSL.

The only modular 2500's were the 2524 and 2525 which just started to
integrated CSU/DSU's for T1's and NT1's for ISDN, but not compatible
with WIC's.

The 2509/2511 make great console servers (8/16 port serial
respectively).  Handling anything higher speed than a couple T1's is
probably not recommended.  They are also very EOL, and 2600's are even
EOL now.

-- 
I'm just a packet pusher.

From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)

Subject: [PLUG] Re: planning for SL5, best path through SL4.4 or FC6?

On Wed, Dec 27, 2006 at 10:03:52AM -0800, Keith Lofstrom wrote:
> 
> So, the question:  If you had to pick a current distro that is closest
> to what SL5 will eventually be, would you start with SL4.2 or with
> Fedora Core 6?  

Since I wrote that, I have been having a fun time porting stuff to
Scientific Linux 4.4, a Red Hat Enterprise Linux clone .   There are
some programs that worked with 2.4.22, and work with >= 2.6.17, but
do not work with 2.6.9 . Fooey!

   However:

I also discovered that RHEL5 beta 2 is available (!) at:

  ftp://ftp.redhat.com/pub/redhat/linux/beta/RHEL5-Beta2/

I made some CDs, and did an install on a spare drive, and RHEL5b2
more or less works.  The gnome logout process does not have a checkbox
for "Save Current Setup", but I've found no other annoyances so far.

So, new plan.  I will duplicate the SL4.4 drive onto the spare drive,
then try an RHEL5 upgrade on that drive and see what breaks.  If that
works, I may continue my upgrade/rebuild work on that drive.  

Many of the RPM packages currently installed by RHEL5 beta 2 still
carry a "fc6" identification, instead of an "el5" identification,
showing the close kinship with Fedora Core 6.  They appear to be
identical otherwise.   Besides some RPM naming differences, el5
and fc6 are about 80% similar, with some minor rev differences
on the remainder.  Very few big changes.

There is 99%+ similarity between RHEL beta 2 and the RPMs for the
"very alpha" SL5 .  Most of the differences are the SL5 extras,
and some are some configuration stuff (like artwork) and docs.
There will probably be more divergence when SL5 is finished.

Questions:  What are the implications when I do the final SL5 upgrade
when that becomes available?  Will RPM consider the package name
differences important, and perhaps fail on the dependencies? 
Anything else to watch out for?

Keith

From heinlein at madboa.com  Tue Jan  9 08:04:23 2007
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Tue, 9 Jan 2007 08:04:23 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Re: planning for SL5, best path through SL4.4 or FC6?
In-Reply-To: <20070109081953.GA21177@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <20070109081953.GA21177@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <alpine.OSX.0.81.0701090756510.17219@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Tue, 9 Jan 2007, Keith Lofstrom wrote:

> Questions:  What are the implications when I do the final SL5 
> upgrade when that becomes available?  Will RPM consider the package 
> name differences important, and perhaps fail on the dependencies? 
> Anything else to watch out for?

I know that CentOS users have typically contributed how-to docs on 
migrating from RHEL to CentOS. I've never used Scientific Linux, but I 
suspect the procedures are rather similar.

The biggest differences between RHEL and its freely rebuilt clones 
have historically been package management and art work. I don't know 
about RHEL5, but its predecessors relied on up2date, while CentOS 
standarized on yum. The transition wasn't horrible, but any mistake is 
costly. Ripping out the Red Hat-copyrighted artwork is usually easier, 
and by now all the clones have their own art styles.

If I were contemplating your move, I'd

1. Install RHEL5 betaX and get everything working
2. Identify and backup the deltas between installation and working
3. Nuke and repave with a fresh SL5 install once it's available
4. Merge deltas backed up in step 2.

But that's just me...

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

From robert.citek at gmail.com  Tue Jan  9 11:58:25 2007
From: robert.citek at gmail.com (Robert Citek)
Date: Tue, 09 Jan 2007 13:58:25 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] root login
In-Reply-To: <45A3EEEB.7060506@verizon.net>
References: <45A3EEEB.7060506@verizon.net>
Message-ID: <45A3F3E1.6000006@gmail.com>

Bruce Kilpatrick wrote:
> robin at moose:~$ sudo bash
> Password:

The above will run bash as root, but with much of your own user
environment.  Instead, try this:

$ sudo su -

This will log you in as root with all of root's environment settings,
i.e. a "real root login"

Regards,
- Robert

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Tue Jan  9 12:05:42 2007

Subject: [PLUG] root login
In-Reply-To: <45A3F3E1.6000006@gmail.com>
References: <45A3EEEB.7060506@verizon.net> <45A3F3E1.6000006@gmail.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0701091203570.22431@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Tue, 9 Jan 2007, Robert Citek wrote:

> $ sudo su -

   You don't need sudo. In a virtual console, type 'su -' and press [Enter].
You'll see a prompt for root's password. Enter that and you have a root
login shell at root's home directory. Using 'su' without the dash raises
your status to that of superuser, but in the current shell.

From robert.citek at gmail.com  Tue Jan  9 12:18:08 2007
From: robert.citek at gmail.com (Robert Citek)
Date: Tue, 09 Jan 2007 14:18:08 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] root login
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0701091203570.22431@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <45A3EEEB.7060506@verizon.net> <45A3F3E1.6000006@gmail.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0701091203570.22431@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <45A3F880.7040509@gmail.com>

Rich Shepard wrote:
> On Tue, 9 Jan 2007, Robert Citek wrote:
> 
>> $ sudo su -
> 
>   You don't need sudo. In a virtual console, type 'su -' and press [Enter].
> You'll see a prompt for root's password. Enter that and you have a root
> login shell at root's home directory. Using 'su' without the dash raises
> your status to that of superuser, but in the current shell.

True for most Unix/Linux.  However, under {Ku,Edu,Xu}buntu and Mac OS X,
there is no root password:

$ sudo grep root /etc/shadow
root:*:13417:0:99999:7:::

So, typing 'su -' alone will not give you a root shell because there is
to password that grants you access to it.  One must use sudo.

As for su alone, that will not run root's startup scripts.  So, if there
are any special configurations to root environment, e.g. from
/root/.profile or /root/.bashrc, those will not get initialized.

Regards,
- Robert

Subject: [PLUG] Starting a POE server at startup
Message-ID: <45A40855.6040902@acm.org>

This is for a Redhat-ian ( Redhat, CentOS, Whitebox, Fedora, etc. ) 
system using the SYSV init process.

I have a POE server I'd like automagically started when the system comes 
up.  What is the preferred method for doing this?

Actually I have no problem creating an init.d script but since this 
simple server doesn't do a lot of fancy stuff at start-up ( capture it's 
and write its pid and so forth ) I thought there might be a better way.

Searches of the web tune up nothing and the POE site doesn't get into this.


TIA,
Rod

From heinlein at madboa.com  Tue Jan  9 14:16:48 2007
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Tue, 9 Jan 2007 14:16:48 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Starting a POE server at startup
In-Reply-To: <45A40855.6040902@acm.org>
References: <45A40855.6040902@acm.org>
Message-ID: <alpine.OSX.0.81.0701091414290.17219@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Tue, 9 Jan 2007, Roderick A. Anderson wrote:

> This is for a Redhat-ian ( Redhat, CentOS, Whitebox, Fedora, etc. ) 
> system using the SYSV init process.
> 
> I have a POE server I'd like automagically started when the system 
> comes up.  What is the preferred method for doing this?
> 
> Actually I have no problem creating an init.d script but since this 
> simple server doesn't do a lot of fancy stuff at start-up ( capture 
> it's and write its pid and so forth ) I thought there might be a 
> better way.
> 
> Searches of the web tune up nothing and the POE site doesn't get 
> into this.

If you aren't going to be starting and stopping this server with any 
regularity, the easiest way to start a process at boot time is adding 
a line or two to /etc/rc.d/rc.local. A process started that way won't 
start/stop when you change runlevels, but it'll always be there after 
bootup.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

From robert.citek at gmail.com  Tue Jan  9 23:44:29 2007

Date: Wed, 10 Jan 2007 01:44:29 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] cable management
Message-ID: <45A4995D.4050203@gmail.com>

Hello all,

I was sitting in wireless cafe the other day and happened to look up at
the ceiling when I noticed a run of ethernet wiring. The wires were not
in a conduit or stapled to the wall, but rather they were held in place
by what I can only describe as metal pig's tails. That is, it looked as
though someone had taken a bolt, twisted one end into a pig's tail (or
corkscrew) and fastened the other end to the wall. The wire was running
through the loop formed by the pig's tail.

Does anyone know what these pig's tail like things are called?

Regards,
- Robert




From jason at jasonjustman.com  Wed Jan 10 00:12:59 2007
From: jason at jasonjustman.com (jason justman)
Date: Wed, 10 Jan 2007 00:12:59 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] cable management
In-Reply-To: <45A4995D.4050203@gmail.com>
References: <45A4995D.4050203@gmail.com>
Message-ID: <45A4A00B.5040109@jasonjustman.com>

i dont have my anixter catalog handy, but they are grouped in the 
'fasteners' section.

they are usually called bridle rings, and are used for small runs of low 
volage cable.. here's an example (with a saddle to help with heavier 
loads - http://www.b-line.com/whatsnew/wn_122.asp ).  other common 
strucuted wiring installation uses j-hooks, raceways/table trays, 
interduct for fiber, etc - don't forget out in p-town you have build for 
seismic bracing.

jason

Message-ID: <20070110191010.GA11582@gate.kl-ic.com>


The Madwifi drivers for Atheros cards seem fairly well supported by
Linux.  I purchased one previously at full price (about $50), and found
some refurbs in plain brown boxes at Frys for $17 ("WG511TNAR", which
means WG511T, North America tuned, refurbished).  For those of you
that want to pester the Linux Clinic with cheap wifi cards, here is
a cheap one that won't get taken outside and stomped.  I bought a
second one for my wife's laptop, and it works pretty much like the
new one.  It says 90 day warranty on the Fry's label on the box, and
30 day warranty on a note inside, so Fry's is, well, Frys ... :-/

I am still having some troubles getting "iwlist" (scanning) working
with the WG511T and a 2.6.9 kernel, but allegedly it will work and I
will get it figured out soon.  The more people that have these cards
locally, the more help in figuring it out (my ulterior motive).

Keith

On Wednesday 10 January 2007 11:10, Keith Lofstrom wrote:
> The Madwifi drivers for Atheros cards seem fairly well supported by
> Linux.  I purchased one previously at full price (about $50), and found

>

> with the WG511T and a 2.6.9 kernel, but allegedly it will work and I
> will get it figured out soon.  The more people that have these cards
> locally, the more help in figuring it out (my ulterior motive).

Atheros-based WICs are great. The newer ones support a/b/g, WPA2, and can be 
used either on the client side or in access points. Atheros opened up their 
drivers (MadWiFi) a few years ago, so they are solid and feature-ful. They 
are dual-licensed, both GPL and BSD.

Both the Atheros web site and madwifi.org have tables that list brand names 
and model numbers of WICs that use Atheros chipsets.

Ralink and Realtek are supposed to be fairly Linux-friendly, and Intel keeps 
making 'we luv Linux' sounds, but so far there's not much to show for all 
that lovin'.

From carla at bratgrrl.com  Wed Jan 10 13:33:08 2007
From: carla at bratgrrl.com (Carla Schroder)
Date: Wed, 10 Jan 2007 13:33:08 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] 100% free wireless cardbus card
Message-ID: <200701101333.08170.carla@bratgrrl.com>

I ran across this on the GNewSense list- the ASUS WL 107g has 100% free 
drivers, there is no binary kernel blob like Atheros uses. The binary blob 
has to do with FCC regulations about not allowing users to dink with the 
broadcast frequencies. (This is the actual rule 
http://ftp.fcc.gov/Bureaus/Engineering_Technology/Orders/2001/fcc01264.pdf) I 
don't know why ASUS can do without the binary firmware blob, but there it is.

Even cooler, the card is cheap, $24.99 on Newegg. It has a Ralink chipset.

From russell at personaltelco.net  Wed Jan 10 14:24:43 2007
From: russell at personaltelco.net (Russell Senior)
Date: 10 Jan 2007 14:24:43 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Inexpensive Netgear WG511T Atheros cards at Frys
In-Reply-To: <200701101253.02376.carla@bratgrrl.com>
References: <20070110191010.GA11582@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<200701101253.02376.carla@bratgrrl.com>
Message-ID: <86vejefrkk.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>

>>>>> "Carla" == Carla Schroder <carla at bratgrrl.com> writes:

Carla> Atheros-based WICs are great. The newer ones support a/b/g,
Carla> WPA2, and can be used either on the client side or in access
Carla> points. Atheros opened up their drivers (MadWiFi) a few years
Carla> ago, so they are solid and feature-ful. They are dual-licensed,
Carla> both GPL and BSD.

Just to rain slightly on your parade, MadWifi drivers leave much to be
desired.  That said, they work, generally.  They are not particularly
"opened up" however.  The current MadWifi drivers rely heavily on a
binary-only HAL, which is kinda-sorta-not-different than binary-blob
firmware except that it runs on your CPU instead of on the card.
Loading Madwifi taints your kernel.  Atheros is apparently not
especially communicative with developers these days, and the currently
available developers are kinda-sorta Not That Competent.  They try.  I
have found that they are particularly not interested in solving
documented configurations (admittedly not the Common Case, but an
important one for us at PTP) on which it is pretty easy to trigger
kernel panics.  Way too easy.  Because of the HAL, it becomes
difficult to fix.  There is some outside work on a branch using the
devicescape 802.11 stack (in place of the net80211 stack ported from
BSD-land that stock MadWifi uses) and also on integrating an OpenHAL.
I haven't tried any of that work yet.  I think you could say it was a
work-in-progress.

Featureful?  Yes.  Solid?  Not so much.

Carla> Both the Atheros web site and madwifi.org have tables that list
Carla> brand names and model numbers of WICs that use Atheros
Carla> chipsets.

Carla> Ralink and Realtek are supposed to be fairly Linux-friendly,
Carla> and Intel keeps making 'we luv Linux' sounds, but so far
Carla> there's not much to show for all that lovin'.

For client-mode, Intel hardware apparently works pretty well.  At
least, I've heard that.  I've got one of their a/b/g radios in a test
rig, but haven't had time to play with it much.  As of 6 months ago,
it was pretty close to impossible to find intel radios retail.  Were
only available as pulls from centrino laptops.  There has been some
work on master-mode on Intel hardware, but my attempts to get it
working have been thwarted so far.  Attempts to ping the developer at
Intel have gone without reply.


-- 
Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net

From russell at personaltelco.net  Wed Jan 10 14:29:36 2007
From: russell at personaltelco.net (Russell Senior)
Date: 10 Jan 2007 14:29:36 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] 100% free wireless cardbus card
In-Reply-To: <200701101333.08170.carla@bratgrrl.com>
References: <200701101333.08170.carla@bratgrrl.com>
Message-ID: <86r6u2frcf.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>

>>>>> "Carla" == Carla Schroder <carla at bratgrrl.com> writes:

Carla> I ran across this on the GNewSense list- the ASUS WL 107g has
Carla> 100% free drivers, there is no binary kernel blob like Atheros
Carla> uses. The binary blob has to do with FCC regulations about not
Carla> allowing users to dink with the broadcast frequencies. (This is
Carla> the actual rule
Carla> http://ftp.fcc.gov/Bureaus/Engineering_Technology/Orders/2001/fcc01264.pdf)
Carla> I don't know why ASUS can do without the binary firmware blob,
Carla> but there it is.

The binary blob has to do with avoiding user-configuration-to-violate-
regulations while using a software-driven radio.  If the card is doing
the regulatory function instead of the driver, then you don't need the
binary blob.  That's the model, anyway.  I don't know anything in
particular about the ASUS device.
