Yeah, you got hacked. What distro and version are you using? What services
were you running before the hack? They could have used a known exploit that
had yet to be patched. They could have used a brute force attack to gain
access. You will want to get your data off that box asap, and reinstall. It
is not likely that you will be able to locate the attacker. The IP address
in Romania is probably another box they rooted, an internet cafe, public
library, college computer lab or something like that. It's not likely that
it's the IP address that their ISP assigned them. Unless you have
potentially lost significant intelectual property, it's not going to worth
persueing. It would be worth finding the exploit they used though, if it's
not too much work, and most people here would probably be interested to
know.

In the future, keep up with updates to the services you are running. Use
good passwords. These two things alone will likely prevent most attacks from
succeeding. Perhaps install an IDS and something like Tripwire routinely
check the integrity of your binaries.

-- 
Rob Anderson
riznob at gmail.com

> > > Ok, so what is going on?
> >
> > You have been rooted.  Those applications (and a few more, I would guess)
> > have been replaced with rigged versions.  (Usually to hide the real apps.)
> >
> >
> > chkrootkit is your friend in this case.
> >
> > > I know I have been compromised and will have to figure out a way to
> > > rebuild the system which isn't easy given the location of the machine.
> > > However, I would really like to know how I was compromised so I can
> > > prevent it in the future.  At the moment, I'm looking at my log files,
> > > but I haven't found much useful information - except that I'm very
> > > suspious of a machine with ip= 81.181.170.26.  It is in Romania.
> >
> > Sound like the IRC twits that rooted my machine about five years back.
> >
> > > Is this a well known event that every security person knows about?
> > > I don't claim to know anything about computer security - well, just
> > > enough to get by - usually - but not today.
> >
> > You will need to reinstall.  Most rootkits install all sorts of backdoors.
> > Finding them all is a royal pain.
> >
> > What version of Linux are you running?
> >
> > If you need help with it, contact me off-line.
> >
> > --
> > "Invoking the supernatural can explain anything, and hence explains
> > nothing."
> >                   - University of Utah bioengineering professor Gregory
> > Clark
> > _______________________________________________
> > PLUG mailing list
> > PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
> > http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug
> >
>
> Yeah, you got hacked. What distro and version are you using? What services
> were you running before the hack? They could have used a known exploit that
> had yet to be patched. They could have used a brute force attack to gain
> access. You will want to get your data off that box asap, and reinstall. It
> is not likely that you will be able to locate the attacker. The IP address
> in Romania is probably another box they rooted, an internet cafe, public
> library, college computer lab or something like that. It's not likely that
> it's the IP address that their ISP assigned them. Unless you have
> potentially lost significant intelectual property, it's not going to worth
> persueing. It would be worth finding the exploit they used though, if it's
> not too much work, and most people here would probably be interested to
> know.
>
> In the future, keep up with updates to the services you are running. Use
> good passwords. These two things alone will likely prevent most attacks from
> succeeding. Perhaps install an IDS and something like Tripwire routinely
> check the integrity of your binaries.
>
> --
> Rob Anderson
> riznob at gmail.com

From russell at personaltelco.net  Fri Jan 19 12:29:49 2007
From: russell at personaltelco.net (Russell Senior)
Date: 19 Jan 2007 12:29:49 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Questions regarding compromised system
In-Reply-To: <45B1195A.3040700@outwardfocus.net>
References: <eac729460701181249k5f5efbe0y2442aed1b98be53f@mail.gmail.com>
	<20070119164132.GA1352@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<eac729460701191103kbac08a5k92ce350ee350b004@mail.gmail.com>
	<45B1195A.3040700@outwardfocus.net>
Message-ID: <86ac0e69qa.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>


FWIW, we recently had a PTP box exploited, apparently using a
vulnerability in Cacti.  At least it got hammered DoS'd into
inoperability.  There are some indications that they were never able
to complete their exploit, because the script they wanted to download
at first couldn't get through the captive portal and/or because the
directory they were trying to copy the script to didn't have
compatible write permissions.

However, we rebooted the machine before we really figured out for sure
what they had managed to do.  Because of the uncertainty, we
reinstalled from scratch, which had the downside (or perverse benefit,
though that is hard to see at the moment) of having to re-figure out
how to configure Cacti for what we wanted to do.  We made a copy of
the compromised system first.

For reasons I don't quite understand, despite a bug being filed about
the vulnerability and now, subsequently resolved, it never got a
mention on the Debian Security Advisory, even to this day.


-- 
Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net

That's the one.  The logs indicate that the exploit was trying to
download a file called ping.txt.  However, our captive portal (on a
different box) was redirecting them to an auth page instead, which
partially thwarted them.  Also, cacti was running as a user that
didn't have write access to the directory where ping.txt would land.
We do have a 1.7 gigabyte /var/log/apache/error.log file to show for
it though.

> That's the one.  The logs indicate that the exploit was trying to
> download a file called ping.txt.  However, our captive portal (on a
> different box) was redirecting them to an auth page instead, which

After having a couple of machines compromised (which I'm not proud to admit)
I did quite a bit of research and found that LCAP is very nice.

http://www.securityfocus.com/tools/882

One of the write ups I followed suggested installing LCAP then running
chkrootkit which has a list of critical binaries it looks at then making
those binaries immutable and then removing the ability to remove the
immutable flag by way of LCAP ... the only way to bypass the security that
creates is physical access and booting into single user (atleast thats the
theory anyway).

So far since I implemented the above as well as several other security
enhancements to my installs I haven't had any trouble :-)

Drew-

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Fri Jan 19 16:39:48 2007
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Fri, 19 Jan 2007 16:39:48 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Sound on ThinkPad 600E: The Final Point
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0701191637230.24251@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

   I didn't mention this on the previous posts, but it is a critical point.
The thinkwiki page said to turn off QuickBoot in the BIOS. Well, I didn't
know whether it was on until I checked. It was. Turning it off allows the
BIOS to see the sound chip(s). Strange, but that's the way it is.

After logging into my Ubuntu Dapper laptop I get 
a second splash screen. This screen shows icons 
loading for Nautilus and a couple other things, 
and then the gnome desktop appears with my panel 
settings and I'm ready to go to work. Well, I 
did something bad and now it the second splash 
screen where the icons for Nautilus, etc. appear 
no longer displays the icons. It sits there for 
a bit, then suddenly I am dropped back to the 
login screen. I can repeat the login all night 
and all it does is cycle me back to the login 
screen. I can hit Ctrl-Alt-F1 and get to a 
command prompt, but I don't know what to do when 
I get there. However, there are other symptoms, 
plus an explanation of what I was doing before 
this sorry state of affairs came to pass.

I was trying to continue making a backup to a 
USB disk hanging on my Windows desktop (which is 
what I am forced to use to write this). A couple 
days ago I finally learned how to mount this 
drive and then attempted to make a backup of my 
entire Ubuntu partition with rsync. That got as 
far as /lib and then the laptop locked up with 
the cpu at 100%. I restarted the laptop and 
continued using it without incident yesterday 
and today. Today I decided to try again. Well, 
this time it got a bit further, and then instead 
of locking up I was suddenly dumped out of the 
GUI and back to the login prompt. Rsync and 
everything else halted. I logged back in and 
everything seemed fine. 

However, while watching the backup I noticed 
that I had quite a few things that I had meant 
to delete. One was a duplicate folder of fonts. 
Another was an iso of Kubuntu Edgy that I had 
burned to CD for someone at the Clinic. I 
deleted the font folder with Nautilus, then 
decided to burn another CD of Kubuntu and then 
delete the 690 MB file. I tried to launch K3b, 
but it wouldn't launch. Then I tried Gnomebaker 
and it wouldn't launch either. In both cases the 
disk light came on and they appeared about ready 
to appear on the desktop, then they disappeared.

Figuring something was amiss because of the 
aborted rsync episode, I decided to shut down 
and restart. When I restarted I found myself at 
the current situation.

At the command line I opened my CheatSheet file 
with nano and found my mount command for the USB 
disk. I mounted it and all the files that rsync 
copied to it are all there, including what 
appears to be everything in /etc/X11. Using ls-l 
I viewed the contents of the /etc/X11 folder on 
the USB disk and on the laptop, and they appear 
to be identical (same file sizes and time 
stamps).

Rsync did not complete the copy process, but it 
did copy /bin, /boot, /dev, /etc, /home, 
/initrd, and at least part of /lib. I haven't 
compared the contents of the USB drive to the 
corresponding folders on the laptop, but if the 
/etc/X11 folder is the same, chances are all the 
rest of the folders it copied are the same as 
well. 

I'm dead out of ideas. I seriously need 
suggestions here!

OK, I may have found something. At the command line I typed 
"startx." That gave me the following:

---------------
xauth: creating new authority file for /jome/jjj/.serverauth.5382
xauth: creating new authority file for /jome/jjj/.serverauth
xauth: creating new authority file for /jome/jjj/.serverauth

Fatal server error
Server is already active for display 0
   If this server is no longer running, remove /tmp/.X0-lock
   and start again

Xlib: Connection to ":0.0" refused by server
Xlib: Invalid MIT-MAGIC-COOKIE-1 key
giving up
xinit: Unable to connect to X server
xinit: No such process (errno 3): Server error
------------------

I am guessing the problem is in the last five lines. Actually, in the 
two Xlib: lines -- something is screwed up in the connection to the 
X server. 

I googled on Invalid MIT-MAGIC-COOKIE and discovered a lot of 
people have had problems with this. Unfortunately, none of the 
suggested solutions that I have found have worked so far.

# from John Jordan
# on Friday 19 January 2007 10:43 pm:

>xauth: error in locking authority file /home/jjj/.Xauthority.

Is that maybe owned by root?  It should be owned by jjj.  If not, you 
may have suffered some file corruption.  I can't tell whether to 
suspect that the lockups and such caused the drive to go down sideways 
or something in the gui+backup, lockup or whatever just accidentally 
put root running X in jjj's environment.  I can't remember whether the 
procedure is to touch the file, or remove it or what.  I can say that 
I've seen it before, but all I remember is that it was a slight speed 
bump.  AFAICT, neither the lock nor the authority file contain anything 
that a fairly healthy system can't figure out for itself.  Probably 
just should have gotten deleted and didn't because of the crash.

--Eric

new filesystem on it, the permissions change to the permissions of the mounted 
filesystem.

I spent from about 8 am to 1 pm on it today, but I still can't log in to 
my account as jjj. It just keeps dumping me back to the login 
screen.

However, if I hit Ctrl-Alt-F1 I can get to the command line, and then 
I can log in as jjj. Unfortunately, everything I have tried at the 
command line has not resolved the problem.

I can also boot to the Recovery option in the Grub menu, which 
gets me to a command line as root. From there I can do "startx" 
and then a GUI comes up. But it is not jjj's GUI, it is root's GUI 
(root's Gnome desktop settings). However, it does have Firefox and 
I can at least google to see if I can find other possible solutions 
without having to use the Windows desktop.

Once, while booting with the Recovery option, the boot process 
discovered a segmentation fault, fsck came up, and went through 
hda2. It didn't report any errors, and the computer thereafter booted 
normally. But maybe there was file corruption. In any event, the 
problem remains, so if the problem was caused by something 
happening to the filesystem, it is still a problem.

The MIT-MAGIC-COOKIE-1 is apparently located in the 
~/.Xauthority file. I noted that this file was owned by root, so I took 
ownership of it as jjj. The /tmp/X0-lock file was also owned by root, 
so I took ownership of it as jjj as well. Both of these are supposed 
to be recreated automatically, so I also tried renaming them. None 
of this helped.

The rsync backup that I did the other day did successfully copy 
everything from /home, so I went there and copied the ~/.Xauthority 
file to the laptop. Still can't log in.

Man xauth lists a lot of options, only some of which were clear to 
me. One was "xauth list," which lists the MIT-MAGIC-COOKIE 
numbers in .Xauthority. I ran this command before and after 
copying the old .Xauthority file from the backup, and afterwards the 
numbers were different. Still no joy, though.

One google site suggested /etc/init.d/gdm stop and then restart. It 
stopped it with [ok], but when I did /etc/init.d/gdm start I got [fail]. I 
suspect it failed for the same reason that it won't start on logging in 
at the GUI login screen. However, I don't clearly understand the 
difference between Gnome display manager (gdm) and X.

Another site suggested that (although dangerous) "$ xhost +local" 
would allow all users in. The command worked without error 
message, but I still can't log in as jjj.

I tried creating a new user (useradd), thinking that I could just 
make /home/jjj the new user's home directory. But Linux puked up 
that idea -- every user needs to have a home folder that is unique 
and can't be written by other users.

Another suggestion that didn't work -- "system-config-display --
reconfig." Just got a "command not found" error.

Suggestions that I haven't tried yet (don't understand them):

dpkg -reconfigure -a
dpkg -reconfigure xdm

I can also note that this problem started the first time I shut down 
and restarted after updating the kernel and nvidia-glx a couple days 
ago. Note that I am using the nv driver, though, not the proprietary 
nvidia driver. At one time I did use the proprietary driver, and 
evidently Update Manager still thinks it needs to be kept updated. I 
went back to the nv driver months ago, so the fact that I changed 
drivers couldn't be related to this problem.

Any other suggestions? Anyone?

I'm off to get a haircut. Maybe with less hair on my head the brain 
will function better.

From dbridges at austin.rr.com  Sat Jan 20 15:22:11 2007
From: dbridges at austin.rr.com (David Bridges)
Date: Sat, 20 Jan 2007 17:22:11 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] X won't start! Help!!
In-Reply-To: <45B20E53.25279.AF6E1EF7@localhost>
References: <45B14978.20001.AC6D277F@localhost>
	<45B20E53.25279.AF6E1EF7@localhost>
Message-ID: <1169335331.13773.8.camel@puzzler.dnbridge.dyndns.org>

> Any other suggestions? Anyone?

If you are using gnome you might try the following.  I have seen
problems in the past with some of my kids accounts and the following
fixed those issues.  I'm sure I could have eventually found the problem,
but figured it was just as easy to let them reconfigure their
desktop(s).

As user jjj from a console login execute the following command and then
try to login via xdm/gdm

mv ~/.gnome2_private ~/old_gnome2_private

This will lose most of your settings related to your desktop.  If this
fixes the problem you can try to dig down and see exactly what
file/folder causes the problem by moving them one at a time from
~/old_gnome2_private to ~/.gnome2_private.

>From what I remember the kids saying this issue happened when they
locked up X and had to ctrl+alt+bs or reset the computer.

-- 
David

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sat Jan 20 16:39:28 2007
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)

Subject: [PLUG] Mount annoyances
In-Reply-To: <20070119190637.dcc90ea6.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20070119190637.dcc90ea6.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <20070121003927.GA16036@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Fri, Jan 19, 2007 at 07:06:37PM -0800, John Jason Jordan wrote:
> I am forever forgetting to mount an external disk before copying or
> moving files to it. Of course, then Linux just copies the files to the
> mount point folder. Then I have to go in, delete them, mount the drive,
> and do the operation over again. Much worse, what if I never realize I
> failed to mount the drive and, thinking I have saved the files, unmount
> the drive and put it away thinking I now have a safe backup?
> 
> The part that is annoying is that Linux will copy the files to the
> mount point. Is there a way to make it never do that? Like, if there is
> no drive actually mounted at the mount point, give me an error message
> when I try to move or copy files there?
> 
> If you haven't already guessed, this just happened again tonight. For
> the eleventy-eleventh time. 

One way to do this is to wrap the mount in a script that renames the
mount point just before mounting it, and another script that renames
it back after unmounting.   That way, there is no directory at the
mount point, under normal conditions.

There might be some things you can do with permissions, too.

From careo at fastmail.fm  Sat Jan 20 20:38:43 2007
From: careo at fastmail.fm (Dane Jensen)
Date: Sat, 20 Jan 2007 20:38:43 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] KVM, virtualization, etc. (Was VMware users group ... )
In-Reply-To: <45B0540D.7070202@cesmail.net>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0701180623070.16729@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<45B03737.9050100@foo-projects.org> <45B0540D.7070202@cesmail.net>
Message-ID: <200701202038.43884.careo@fastmail.fm>

On Thursday 18 January 2007 21:15, M. Edward (Ed) Borasky wrote:
> Well ... the ability to boot a LiveCD on a machine in a store rather
> limits you to stores where the sales people aren't on commission. :) But
> seriously, it would be easier to read the ads, check the chips out on
> the Internet and buy something you know will work. Or go someplace where
> they build them to order like Fry's or CompUSA.

Most new Intel and AMD chips that don't target the lowend of the market will 
have either one of their hardware virtualization setups built-in. 

From http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virtualization_Technology
"It is available on most Pentium 4 6x2, Pentium D 9x0, Xeon 3xxx/5xxx/7xxx, 
Core Duo and Core 2 Duo processors (excluding the T5200 & T5500). On some 
implementations, IVT support may be switched off in the BIOS/EFI."

"AMD processors using Socket AM2, Socket S1, and Socket F include AMD 
Virtualization support. In May 2006, AMD introduced such versions of the 
Athlon 64, Turion 64, and 64-bit Sempron processors. AMD Virtualization is 
also supported by release two (x2xx series) of the Opteron processors."

If you have any doubts about a particular processor, the Intel or AMD sites 
are somewhat useful, although it's not a heavily advertised feature so you 
have to go digging into the detailed specs.

I know in my own limited experience, it makes a noticeable improvement in 
Parallels on my Macbook Pro to turn VT on.

HTH,
-Dane

Special Topic today:  Setting up VMware and Windoze virtualizers.  If
you still need to run a few offbeat windoze apps, and they don't work
with Wine or Crossover Office, VMware is a fairly easy way to run a
copy of Windoze in a virtual window.  To install it on your own machine,
you will need Windoze install disks and a valid 25 digit license key; if
you want to violate copyright laws we don't want to know about it.  If
you have another virtualizer that will also run Windoze on older
hardware, that will also be interesting.

From ehem at m5p.com  Sun Jan 21 13:29:11 2007
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Sun, 21 Jan 2007 13:29:11 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] X won't start! Help!!
In-Reply-To: <20070121163010.AC59541FF@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200701212129.l0LLTBE3091104@m5p.com>

>From: "John Jordan" <johnxj at comcast.net>
> I spent from about 8 am to 1 pm on it today, but I still can't log in to 
> my account as jjj. It just keeps dumping me back to the login 
> screen.
> 
> However, if I hit Ctrl-Alt-F1 I can get to the command line, and then 
> I can log in as jjj. Unfortunately, everything I have tried at the 
> command line has not resolved the problem.

So the X-server starts up, this is merely a problem with XDM/the desktop
environment? (you get the graphical login box, just you get kicked back
out?)

> I can also boot to the Recovery option in the Grub menu, which 
> gets me to a command line as root. From there I can do "startx" 
> and then a GUI comes up. But it is not jjj's GUI, it is root's GUI 
> (root's Gnome desktop settings). However, it does have Firefox and 
> I can at least google to see if I can find other possible solutions 
> without having to use the Windows desktop.

This means running your desktop environment as root. This is a bad idea
as it is complex software not really written to run as root. In
particular graphical web browsers are a distinct no-no as root.

> Once, while booting with the Recovery option, the boot process 
> discovered a segmentation fault, fsck came up, and went through 
> hda2. It didn't report any errors, and the computer thereafter booted 
> normally. But maybe there was file corruption. In any event, the 
> problem remains, so if the problem was caused by something 
> happening to the filesystem, it is still a problem.

This would likely hit ephemeral files, not ones that stick around. Still
something to be wary of.

> The MIT-MAGIC-COOKIE-1 is apparently located in the 
> ~/.Xauthority file. I noted that this file was owned by root, so I took 
> ownership of it as jjj. The /tmp/X0-lock file was also owned by root, 
> so I took ownership of it as jjj as well. Both of these are supposed 
> to be recreated automatically, so I also tried renaming them. None 
> of this helped.

~/.Xauthority should be owned by you, it is also ephemeral. Deleting it
is safe, just in-operation X sessions will have problems. /tmp/.X0-lock
is also ephemeral and should be owned by root (it is the X-server making
sure not to collide with other instances).

> One google site suggested /etc/init.d/gdm stop and then restart. It
> stopped it with [ok], but when I did /etc/init.d/gdm start I got [fail]. I
> suspect it failed for the same reason that it won't start on logging in
> at the GUI login screen. However, I don't clearly understand the
> difference between Gnome display manager (gdm) and X.

X is refering to the X-server, the piece of software that actually draws
things on your screen. gdm/XDM are X-clients that tell the X-server what
to draw. Notably they tell the X-server to draw a box on the screen with
the words "login", "username" and "password"; as with `login` they then
switch to the appropriate user and run other X-clients as specified by
the user's environment.

> Another site suggested that (although dangerous) "$ xhost +local"
> would allow all users in. The command worked without error
> message, but I still can't log in as jjj.

Unlikely. This only effects the current session, it would allow you to
run X-clients and have them draw on the display, but it wouldn't change
whether you could login.

> Suggestions that I haven't tried yet (don't understand them):
> 
> dpkg -reconfigure -a
> dpkg -reconfigure xdm

Simply run those commands is what is ment. They reach into Debian's
package manager (which Ubuntu uses) all tells it to run through the
configurations and try to redo them.


>From the sounds of things, try these steps:

Remove ~/.Xauthority (it will be recreated)
Rename ~/.xinitrc ~/.xsession and ~/.xclients if they exist, these allow
users to change the environment in interesting ways. They're a likely
source of problems.

Boot to single-user mode ("Recovery mode"), and remove everything in
/tmp. Make sure not to remove the directory /tmp, just all of the
contents thereof (if you accidentally do that, the ownership is
root:root, and the mode is 1777).

Try logging in, if it still fails, look at the contents of
~/.xsession-errors if that points you at something you can fix, do so.
If not, report the contents.

On Sun, 21 Jan 2007 16:50:47 -0600 (CST)
David Fleck <david.fleck at mchsi.com> dijo:

> On Fri, 19 Jan 2007, John Jordan wrote:
> > Deleted /tmp/.X-lock and now I get:
> > 
> > xauth: error in locking authority file /home/jjj/.Xauthority.

> What does 'ls -l /home/jjj/.Xauthority' produce?

The .Xauthority file was there. I also recreated it, about a hundred
times. I googled all over the net and found dozens and dozens of sites
relating to the "Invalid MIT-MAGIC-COOKIE -1" error. None of the
suggestions worked. I spent about ten hours in all trying to get my
login back. I could log in using the Recovery option and, as root, I
could do startx. I could also boot the regular version (which required
my jjj login) and instead of logging in go to a command line with
Ctrl-Alt-F1. But even if I sudo-ed things I could not get the gdm
screen to come up when I was jjj. I would always get bumped back to the
graphical login screen.

About 8:30 last night I finally gave up. I was running out of web sites
with suggestions. I had posted questions on Ubuntu forums, but had no
responses. I decided I had no choice but to reinstall. That meant a
couple more days lost reinstalling and reconfiguring gnome and
applications. As a last ditch before reinstalling I decided to try a
dist-upgrade. I was hoping that doing so would fix the problem, yet
preserve my existing settings and installed applications. It took a
couple hours with my home cable connection, but when it was finished I
was able to log in again as jjj. Yay! 

A few things were broken, but they looked like they'd be easy to fix --
a couple launch icons on the gnome panel, for example. Indeed, things
didn't look too bad. But then I discovered that it broke the wireless.
Wireless broke over a year ago upgrading from Hoary to Breezy, and
again later upgrading from Breezy to Dapper, so breaking the wireless
was not surprising. However, this time I could not get it working. The
instructions failed at a make command. And today at the Clinic I
discovered that the upgrade has perpetuated something that has
apparently been broken for some time -- my Grub boot menu. There is
another bare-bones and never used installation of Ubuntu Dapper on
hda3, and evidently the Grub menu item that I am booting to in order to
launch the real installation on hda2 is actually reading the boot menu
on hda3. Hence, although the upgrade installed kernel 2.6.17, I am
booting to 2.6.15. But the make command is looking for a dfferent
kernel. Not finding it, it fails.

So my next activity will be to fix Grub. That may be a challenge,
considering how borked it appears to be. In the meantime, the computer
is running with my normal login, all programs are still working (sort
of) and all my files are intact.

Thanks to all for the suggestions, and especially to Keith at the
Clinic today for helping figure out where the problem is.

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Mon Jan 22 09:19:37 2007
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Mon, 22 Jan 2007 09:19:37 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Mount annoyances
In-Reply-To: <45B242C7.6050608@foo-projects.org>
References: <20070119190637.dcc90ea6.johnxj@comcast.net>
	<45B19BC4.3090309@gmail.com>  <45B242C7.6050608@foo-projects.org>
Message-ID: <1169486377.22216.11.camel@willow.odshp.com>

On Sat, 2007-01-20 at 08:26 -0800, Auke Kok wrote:

> should be as simple as making the mount point read-only. As soon as you mount a 
> new filesystem on it, the permissions change to the permissions of the mounted 
> filesystem.

Indeed; and a little more hand-holdingly:

# chmod 0555 /whatever

It may not be entirely fool-proof; if you're doing the copying as root,
it could very well ignore the permissions and let you write--that's why
it's a superuser.

Before you do this, you might find it helpful to touch a file with a
name like 'filesystem-is-not-mounted', so you'll know immediately if it
is or isn't.

Wil

Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>

   When I ran 2.x kernels I would synchronize my PDA with jpilot using
/dev/ttyS0 or it's alias, /dev/pilot. Now that I have a 2.6.x kernel
installed I cannot sync the PDA.

   Checking /dev I see that ttyS0 is now a symlink to tts/0. But, changing
the jpilot setting and the softlink to that name does not permit the PDA to
communicate with the desktop. It just sits there until it times out.

   What do you suggest as a device I could use? /dev/pilot->/dev/tts/0
doesn't work and neither does /dev/pilot->/dev/ttyS0.

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Mon Jan 22 14:25:45 2007
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Mon, 22 Jan 2007 14:25:45 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Checking File Integrity with PGP Signature
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0701221403510.30116@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0701121230220.810@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<55cf303a0701171249g32f14ba4m1ccc2185bcb80a6c@mail.gmail.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0701221403510.30116@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <1169504745.22216.34.camel@willow.odshp.com>

On Mon, 2007-01-22 at 14:07 -0800, Rich Shepard wrote:
>    Then, when I use your syntax above I get
> 
> [rshepard at salmo /opt]$ gpg --verify scribus-1.3.3.7.tar.bz2.sig 
> gpg: Signature made Tue 09 Jan 2007 11:37:57 AM PST using DSA key ID
> F60166DA
> gpg: Can't check signature: public key not found
> 
>    So, is there a way to determine if the file's been changed? Or, is the
> addition of the signature just for show?

You'll need the signer's public key in your keyring; try adding
'--keyserver hkp://subkeys.pgp.net' to the command line.  Alternately,
you can find and download the user's public key and import it with
'--import'.

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
http://nakedape.cc

>
> -----BEGIN PGP SIGNATURE-----
> Version: GnuPG v1.4.5 (GNU/Linux)
>
> iD8DBQBFo+8V73uV5/YBZtoRAmskAJ9AX+AAVvtl/Tq7G5396hVojsuP9wCfUnZh

> F60166DA
> gpg: Can't check signature: public key not found
>
>  So, is there a way to determine if the file's been changed? Or, is the
> addition of the signature just for show?

The key for the file is not in your keyring.  You need to edit the 
.gnupg/gpg.conf file and uncomment the line with "keyserver-options 
auto-key-retrieve".  GPG is not able to automatically retrieve the keys.

From dwight.hubbard at gmail.com  Mon Jan 22 18:03:47 2007
From: dwight.hubbard at gmail.com (Dwight Hubbard)
Date: Mon, 22 Jan 2007 18:03:47 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Need Help in SuSEfirewall2/Iptables
In-Reply-To: <a49cba240701191529x6122c79dl742930fcbf2bdb28@mail.gmail.com>
References: <a49cba240701191529x6122c79dl742930fcbf2bdb28@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <321871a60701221803l30690bb1gb44d095c5c1c1026@mail.gmail.com>

in /etc/hosts.deny put:
sshd: ALL

in /etc/hosts.allow put the network to allow for example to allow
192.168.* and 184.23.35.26 it would look like this:
sshd: 192.168., 184.23.35.26

friends who are mostly Mac folk, and use it to get together on line.
They want me to join them.

I went to http://secondlife.com/ to have a look around and found that
they have clients for XP and OS-X, so I went to their contact page and
started a question about when they'd have a Linux client. I was
impressed to find that their contact page is "smarter" than a simple
e-mail entry form. After clicking the submit button, they returned a
page that said that they didn't actually submit my question, yet, and
gave me a list of links, with abstracts, that their engine thinks are
related to my question. The first one was a link to a page offering an
alpha client. They have caveats about using it -- something to do with
changes made from the alpha client being permanent. I still don't know
enough about SL to know what that means, but I'm glad to be warned up front.

I'll be giving SL a try sometime next month. Meanwhile, if anyone's
tried the alpha client, I'd like to know about any other gotchas.

From johnxj at comcast.net  Mon Jan 22 22:44:39 2007
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Mon, 22 Jan 2007 22:44:39 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Godawful Grub mess
Message-ID: <20070122224439.5e666ee0.johnxj@comcast.net>

While trying to install ndiswrapper at the Clinic Sunday I discovered
that my Grub boot menu.lst files are a hopeless mess. I need to fix it
just on general principles, but also because I am incapable of
installing ndiswrapper until I get the right kernel booted.

First, some basic facts:

There are three partitions:
	hda1 - 1 GB swap
	hda2 - 60 GB, my real installation, upgraded on 1/17 to Edgy
	hda3 - 8 GB, an unused installation of Dapper, bare bones.

Both installations are Ubuntu amd64. Each automatically mounts the
other's partition at boot. While booted into in my real installation
(hda2), I opened the /boot/grub/menu.lst files on each partition with
Gedit, printed them out, and also copied and pasted them into an OOo
document which I exported to PDF for reference purposes. I also saved
each file as menu.lst.backup_1/22/2007.

The problem:

The menu.lst file on hda2 lists eight options. The menu.lst file on
hda3 lists 16 options. The actual menu I see at boot time lists 16
options (see below). However, four of the 16 options I see at boot time
boot me to the hda2 installation, four boot me to the hda3
installation, and the other eight boot to hda3 but are broken (mouse
and keyboard dead). In each case the options come in groups of four.
The following was painstakingly written down from the boot menu I see
on the screen, and will illustrate what I mean by "groups of four." For
convenience I have numbered them. To keep each option all on one line,
U=Ubuntu, k=kernel, rm=(recovery mode) and hda2=(on /dev/hda2).

------
1)
U k 2.6.15-27-amd64-generic
U k 2.6.15-27-amd64-generic (rm)
U k 2.6.15-23-amd64-generic
U k 2.6.15-23-amd64-generic (rm
U memtest 86+

Other operating systems
2) 
U k 2.6.15-26-amd64-generic (hda2)
U k 2.6.15-26-amd64-generic (rm) (hda2)
U k 2.6.15-25-amd64-generic (hda2)
U k 2.6.15-25-amd64-generic (rm) (hda2)
U memtest 86+ (hda2)

3)
U k 2.6.15-26-amd64-generic (hda2)
U k 2.6.15-26-amd64-generic (rm) (hda2)
U k 2.6.15-25-amd64-generic (hda2)
U k 2.6.15-25-amd64-generic (rm) (hda2)
U memtest 86+ (hda2)

4)
U k 2.6.15-26-amd65-generic
U k 2.6.15-26-amd65-generic (rm)
U k 2.6.15-25-amd65-generic
U k 2.6.15-25-amd65-generic (rm)
U memtest 86+
-----

Now, the weird part is that (1), (2) and (3) boot to hda3, although (2)
and (3) are broken. Yes, I know (2) and (3) say "hda2" but they
definitely boot to hda3. And (4) is the only one that boots to hda2.
Yes, I know it doesn't say "hda2," but that's what it boots to. And,
while (4) boots to hda2, it should list kernel 2.6.17-10-generic, since
that is what the upgrade to Edgy installed. (And 2.6.17-10 is definitely
there, although I am not able to boot to it.)

I have some suspicions how this mess came about (either a corrupted
hard disk or buggy upgrade utilities from Ubuntu, or both), but I am
less concerned with how it happened than how to fix it.

Over the past 24 hours I have read quite a few docs on Grub, but a lot
remains unclear. For one thing, how does Grub know exactly which kernel
to boot? Sure, the kernel is listed in the menu, but some of those
kernels exist in both partitions and it is choosing the kernel on the
wrong partition. In fact, how does it even know which menu.lst file to
display?


From tonyr at hevanet.com  Tue Jan 23 08:00:02 2007
From: tonyr at hevanet.com (Tony Rick)
Date: Tue, 23 Jan 2007 08:00:02 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Godawful Grub mess
In-Reply-To: <20070122224439.5e666ee0.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20070122224439.5e666ee0.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <45B63102.3010304@hevanet.com>

John,

I would help a lot if you could post the actual menu.list file/files
contents. 

Remember that the boot/root devices are actually specified by the 'hd(n,m)'
specifier, where hd(0,1) means hda2, and hd(0,2) means hda3.

The menu.lst file is determined when you install grub.  Which of you
installations was the most recent?

One of the installation parameters specifies which device has the grub 
directory
of interest.   During installation grub looks around for other candidate
OS and creates entries for them. You seem to have four different
versions of the kernel (-23, -25, -26, -27) spread across two devices.
If you look in hda2/boot and hda3/boot you shoud see several versions
of the kernel there.  (there are soft links to them in the / directory).

This may be old news to you.  Copies of the menu.list files would really
help.

- tony

They are kind of long to post here, and can't send attachments to the
list. But I sent them as an attachment to you off-list.

> Remember that the boot/root devices are actually specified by the 'hd(n,m)'
> specifier, where hd(0,1) means hda2, and hd(0,2) means hda3.

This I was already aware of.

> The menu.lst file is determined when you install grub.  Which of you
> installations was the most recent?

I think this is part of the source of the problem. First I had an
installation on hda2 of Breezy, and hda3 did not yet exist. I tried to
upgrade it to Dapper and the apt-get dist-upgrade hopelessly borked it.
After a week of trying to fix unmet dependencies I created hda3 on an
unused portion of the disk and installed a fresh Dapper on it. I
continued to use this and eventually ended up recreating everything I
had originally had on the now-borked Breezy-Dapper hda2. But hda3 is
only 8 GB and I needed the space (60 GB) of hda2. So after a month of
working only in the hda3 Dapper, I reformatted hda2, then rsynced hda3
to it. Unfortunately, this resulted in a corrupted filesystem. Later I
discovered that the filesystem corruption was probably caused by a
flaky hard drive connection (since fixed, I hope). In fact, it may have
already been corrupt at the time I rsncyed hda3 to hda2. After fsck-ing
both partitions the filesystems were back to normal and I was able to
use the hda2 installation. After I was satisfied that it was OK, I
wiped out hda3 and installed a fresh Dapper on it just to use as a
rescue installation. Afterwards I ended up with the menu I posted
earlier. 

As you can see, the problem was caused by 1) user incompetence, 2)
filesystem corruption and (possibly), 3) install/update utilities that
didn't operate quite the way they should have.

> One of the installation parameters specifies which device has the grub 
> directory
> of interest.   During installation grub looks around for other candidate
> OS and creates entries for them. You seem to have four different
> versions of the kernel (-23, -25, -26, -27) spread across two devices.
> If you look in hda2/boot and hda3/boot you shoud see several versions
> of the kernel there.  (there are soft links to them in the / directory).

It is now morning and the first cup of coffee is starting to work. I
now realize that Linux cannot boot from two menu.lst files. It is using
one or the other. Since the one on hda3 is the latest to be installed,
and since it has 16 entries, the same number as I see on the
screen during boot, that is probably the one that is being used.
Theory: The upgrade of hda2 to Edgy probably updated that one (hda3)
and did not touch the one on hda2, because it would realize that the
menu.lst file on hda3 is the one that was actually used to boot. 

Unfortunately, while that sounds logical to me, the actual entries in
the two menu.lst files does not bear this out. The upgrade to Edgy
definitely added the 2.6.17-10 kernel to the installation on hda2, but
that kernel appears only in the menu.lst file on hda2. In fact, it is
the first entry in the hda2 menu.lst file. Yet the 2.6.17-10 kernel
does not appear on the screen during boot. So it may be that the
upgrade to Edgy modified the menu.lst file on hda2 (thinking that it
was the correct menu.lst file to change, since that was the
installation that was being upgraded), when it should have upgraded the
menu.lst file on hda3. At least that is my current theory.

At this point I think I need to:

1) Figure out which kernels are available to boot to in each
installation -- and maybe do some tidying up of kernels, since I think
I have a surplus of kernels in each installation.

2) Take the menu.lst file from hda2 (remember I have backup copies of
both originals) and edit out all the current list entries to make entries
for just the kernel options for each installation, then make Grub use
this menu.lst file.

To learn: 

1) How to figure out which kernels are available in each installation,
which ones I should save (just the most recent two, I would presume),
and how to get rid of (uninstall) the others. One of the things I don't
understand is the difference between "image," "headers," "kernel,"
"source," and the vmlinuz* files that I see scattered around. I have
known for some time that the boot menu had some entries that were
broken, but as long as there was one that worked for each installation
I was too lazy to do anything about it. The current learning exercise
is happening because I discovered (after upgrading hda2 to Edgy) that I
cannot reinstall ndiswrapper (broken by the upgrade) because the make
command won't work. I need to figure which
kernel-image-headers-source-vlinuz* parts I need in order to get the
make command to work on hda2 Edgy.

2) How to edit the Grub menu.lst file (the docs I already have should
be adequate for this).

3) How to make Linux use the new menu.lst file to boot from (probably
also in the docs).

Today I'll have most of the afternoon free to work on this. Meantime,
any further comments, suggestions and observations are welcome. Thanks
for the suggestions so far. :)

I have a perl socket program called setdodoip.pl.

I want to make sure it is running from a bash script.


There is the option of doing:

`ps ax|grep 'setdodoip.pl'|tr -s ' '|cut -d ' ' -f1`

to get a process id if setdodoip.pl is running.


Hmm, isn't there an easier, generic, and more reliable
way to check if a program is running than trying to
filter the process id of it from the output of the ps
command?

starts.  Trouble is, that file could still exist when
setdodoip.pl isn't running.  In the perl script, can I
add deleting the file I create when the process is

Be warned that 'pidof' might not be in your path. On redhat-ish
distros it is installed as /sbin/pidof, and non-root users don't
normally have the /sbin directory in their PATH.

From tonyr at hevanet.com  Tue Jan 23 11:21:52 2007
From: tonyr at hevanet.com (Tony Rick)
Date: Tue, 23 Jan 2007 11:21:52 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Godawful Grub mess
In-Reply-To: <20070123094515.61348522.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20070122224439.5e666ee0.johnxj@comcast.net>	<45B63102.3010304@hevanet.com>
	<20070123094515.61348522.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <45B66050.3040200@hevanet.com>

John,

Sounds like you are on the right track. Here are a few points.

1. The hda3 menu.list actually contains about 20 entries. There are
several between the 'AUTOMAGIC' comment lines, and several after it.
One of the comments in the menu.list file is about option 'howmany',
and indicates that only the FIRST instance of a repeated kernel spec is
displayed in the grub boot list. Some of the kernels are repeated in 
menu.list, so you only see 16 at boot time. (There are about 4 for
'memtest86').
I'm not sure what the criteria are for 'repeated', but it obviously
applies.

2. Grub is going to continue to boot from hda3 (the most recent install 
target) until you force it to do otherwise. One way is to
re-install the grub boot block from a
booted hda2. That should tell grub to look for menu.list on hda2.
You will need to have an existing 'menu.list' file in directory
/boot/grub. Look at commands 'grub-install' (installs the boot block)
and 'update-grub' (recreates menu.list). You can exercise finer grain
control with 'grub-install' if you want to, but you really need to know
more about grub before you do that.

3. Some 'grub links' that I have used (I know how you like research)
http://users.bigpond.net.au/hermanzone/p15.htm
http://www.troubleshooters.com/linux/grub/grub.htm
http://www.gnu.org/software/grub/manual/html_node/index.html#Top

4. A clean install will automatically do the bootloader (grub/lilo)
installation unless you skip that step. I use the alternate install CD,
which allows you to bail out at any point (that '<Go Back>' option is
reachable with the tab key), or you can simply refuse all boot loader
options (this as a problem avoidance technique for future multiple
OS installations.)

5. There are usually two places that the kernel files show up. The
originals are in /boot, and / has soft links to some of them. There are
five files of interest for each kernel: a 'vmlinuz' file, an 'initrd' 
file, and
'abi' file, a 'config' file and a 'System.map'. Only the 'vmlinuz' and
'initrd' files are linked in /. You should remove all of those that you
don't need, and remove any references to them in menu.list.

- tony

* shell access via ssh (I read all mail with mutt) to a Linux or *BSD
  host

* web hosting (my sites are non-commercial, so low bandwidth limits ok,
  but one of them will occasionally chew some CPU cycles)

* cgi can be perl, and, ideally, self-built binaries

* tech support with a clue, but needn't respond immediately

* cost appropriate for non-commercial use: <= $15/month.

* bonus if they're in the NW and could be my access point from Qwest
  DSL, saving me the $10/month I'm paying Qwest for "ISP" services
  I don't use.

date.  Actually, I already have everything I need with one exception.

On 1/23/07, Tony Rick <tonyr at hevanet.com> wrote:

> 5. There are usually two places that the kernel files show up. The
> originals are in /boot, and / has soft links to some of them. There are
> five files of interest for each kernel: a 'vmlinuz' file, an 'initrd'
> file, and
> 'abi' file, a 'config' file and a 'System.map'. Only the 'vmlinuz' and
> 'initrd' files are linked in /. You should remove all of those that you
> don't need, and remove any references to them in menu.list.


Just a comment:  Remove the kernels from menu.lst *first*.  It doesn't
matter if the files are on disk or not, aside from the aesthetics of running
'ls' in / and /boot, assuming everything works.

After getting the grub menu behaving correctly, and after getting
ndiswrapper working, and anything else you run into, then delete the extra
kernels.  Until you get everything working, you never know, one of those
kernels might be the one you need.  (or you might delete the wrong one by
accident).

>From your prior messages, it's apparent that you keep immaculate backups,
but it never hurts to approach a problem in such a way that the possibility
of error is minimized.

--Rogan

On Tue, 23 Jan 2007 12:37:50 -0800
"Rogan Creswick" <creswick at gmail.com> dijo:

> Just a comment:  Remove the kernels from menu.lst *first*.  It doesn't
> matter if the files are on disk or not, aside from the aesthetics of running
> 'ls' in / and /boot, assuming everything works.

This strikes me as an excellent suggestion. I did verify that my "real"
installation of Edgy on hda2 has all the files indicated by Tony
previously for:

2.6.17-10-Generic
2.6.15-27-amd64-Generic
2.6.15-26-amd64-Generic
2.6.15-25-amd64-Generic

The first one is the one installed by Edgy and, therefore, the one I
want to list first as the default to boot.

I edited the menu.lst file on hda2 and made it say the above list, plus:

2.6.15-27-amd64-Generic on hda3

I don't much care about the Dapper installation on hda3 because it has
never really been used. Push come to shove I can reinstall it with no
loss. I know it has both the -27 and -23 kernels, but I added just the
-27 to the menu.lst to keep it short and simple.

I have not touched the menu.lst file on hda3. After saving the new
menu.lst in /boot/grub on hda2 I restarted the computer. The menu list
is identical to what it was before -- proof that it is using the
menu.lst file from hda3.

Now I need to make Grub use the menu.lst file on hda2. I have read the
official Grub manual, read man grub, read grub info, googled for an
hour, and I'm not sure what command to enter to do that. Closest I can
find is a website that suggested:

grub> install (hd0,1)/grub/stage1 d (hd0) (hd0,1)/boot/grub/stage2 p (hd0,1)/grub/menu.lst

Unfortunately, said web site gave zero explanation of what any of the
above does. I am guessing that it makes Stage 1 on the MBR of hda, then
tells Stage 1 to display menu.lst from /boot/grub on hda2. But I don't
have any idea what the "d" and "p" do in the line.

It's hard to find documentation for what I need to do because 99% of
the Grub documentation assumes there has never been more than one
menu.lst file.

As usual, suggestions welcome.


From merlyn at stonehenge.com  Tue Jan 23 21:39:28 2007
From: merlyn at stonehenge.com (Randal L. Schwartz)
Date: Tue, 23 Jan 2007 21:39:28 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Re: Check program running...
References: <20070122132128.2s3y6t6lckgwg8sc@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <8664axx9tb.fsf@blue.stonehenge.com>

>>>>> "plug" == plug 0 <plug_0 at robinson-west.com> writes:

plug> One thought is to have setdodoip.pl create a file when it
plug> starts.  Trouble is, that file could still exist when
plug> setdodoip.pl isn't running.  In the perl script, can I
plug> add deleting the file I create when the process is
plug> killed or doesn't start?

Just have it flock a file exclusively.  Then try to get that flock, because
if you can, the program is gone.

See examples of Perl flock at my "highlander" solution ("there can be only
one!") at <http://www.stonehenge.com/merlyn/WebTechniques/col54.html>.

From tonyr at hevanet.com  Tue Jan 23 23:31:11 2007
From: tonyr at hevanet.com (Tony Rick)

Subject: [PLUG] Grub mess - almost there
In-Reply-To: <20070123211608.68426159.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20070122224439.5e666ee0.johnxj@comcast.net>	<45B63102.3010304@hevanet.com>	<20070123094515.61348522.johnxj@comcast.net>	<45B66050.3040200@hevanet.com>	<c834ec80701231237q7bcf5079gd13211b4a5223eef@mail.gmail.com>
	<20070123211608.68426159.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <45B70B3F.1070109@hevanet.com>

The explanation for that command line is at
http://www.gnu.org/software/grub/manual/html_node/install.html#install

That is actually a pretty good manual. It also suggests that you don't
use the 'install' command, but instead use the 'setup' command.
Here <http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=224351> is a Ubuntu Forum 
post from someone who did it that way. The
thread is about installing from a LiveCD, but the method is general
(i.e., doesn't matter where you do it from as long as you booted into
linux somewhere and the grub utilites are installed). You should
verify that the grub stuff is really installed on hda2; I assume that
is your desired root device.

Here is another suggestion. Copy the 2.6.17-10 stanzas (stanza means
everything between 'title' and 'boot', inclusive) from the hda2 menu.list
to the hda3 menu.list, at the bottom. Change hd(0,2) wherever it exists 
in those
stanzas to hd(0,1). (I'm assuming here that the 2.6.17-10 kernel really
is on hda2). Change those ugly-looking root=<nasty-id-string> things
to root=/dev/hda2. Reboot. The 2.6.17-10 kernel selections should appear 
in the boot menu now. Select one, then do the grub
install business. Then do the cleanup business.

- tony

# from Rich Shepard
# on Tuesday 23 January 2007 04:27 pm:

>plugin: failed to parse plugin /etc/mail/spamassassin/FuzzyOcr.pm:
> Can't locate FuzzyOcr/Logging.pm in @INC (@INC contains:
> /etc/mail/spamassassin /usr/lib/perl5/site_perl/5.8.8/i486-linux
> /usr/lib/perl5/site_perl/5.8.8 /usr/lib/perl5/5.8.8/i486-linux
> /usr/lib/perl5/5.8.8
>/usr/lib/perl5/site_perl) at /etc/mail/spamassassin/FuzzyOcr.pm line
> 24. BEGIN failed--compilation aborted at
> /etc/mail/spamassassin/FuzzyOcr.pm line 24.

Hi Rich,

That does sound odd.  @INC is where perl looks for modules, and it does 
appear that your program has added that directory to @INC.  The "failed 
to parse plugin" message hints that there's an eval somewhere trying to 
load the plugin (which hints that it might be trying to do something 
else fancy and getting that wrong), but I can't see exactly what's 
wrong from that error.

What does this give you?
  perl -I/etc/mail/spamassassin/ -e 'use FuzzyOcr::Logging'
or
  perl -I/etc/mail/spamassassin/ -e 'use FuzzyOcr'

And, just to be sure
  ls /etc/mail/spamassassin/FuzzyOcr/Logging.pm

If none of those complains, then the frontend is bringing this trouble 
on itself with some black magic gone bad or something.  Maybe a bad 
install, or incompatible versions?

--Eric

On Tue, 23 Jan 2007 23:31:11 -0800
Tony Rick <tonyr at hevanet.com> dijo:

> The explanation for that command line is at
> http://www.gnu.org/software/grub/manual/html_node/install.html#install

I actually have that manual printed out. But I couldn't understand much
of it.

However, I also have a couple other Linux books here, including Carla's
Cookbook. She has quite a few pages on Grub. It took a bit of trial and
error, but I finally managed to get it set to use the menu.lst file
in /boot/grub on hda2 instead of the one on hda3. Yay!

[Side comment: The reason it took me a long time is because you have to
do "sudo grub" before the grub commands will all work. I was just doing
"grub," the grub prompt would come up, and some commands would work,
but not others.]

When I restarted the new menu came up. I had changed the colors to blue
and white so it was immediately obvious that I finally had the
menu.lst on hda2. However, when I tried to boot any of my new entries
all I could get was the installation on hda3, and that installation was
sometimes screwed up -- e.g., it is still just Dapper, but with some of
the boot entries the Edgy splash screen would appear.

Eventually I found the error. I had used the original menu.lst file in
hda2 as the starting point, since it had the 2.6.17-10 kernel at the
top of the list. I copied and pasted the entries for the Dapper
installation in the menu.lst on hda3 into the menu.lst on hda2 at the
end. When I tried the new entries these last ones were the only ones
that worked properly -- that is, they booted properly into Dapper on
hda3. Reviewing the various entries I noticed that these entries all
said "root=/dev/hda3 quiet no splash." The others all had strange long
numbers starting with "UUID." I replaced the long numbers with the
appropriate "root=" text like the entries for Dapper on hda3, and
voil?! I am currently happily booted into my new Edgy upgrade. As soon
as it came up I heaved a sigh of relief. Then I did uname -r and was
ecstatic to see "2.6.17-10-generic." Yay!

Then I went back to the ndiswrapper problem and ran the make command.
It worked! Yay!

However, for some reason ndiswrapper will not work. I've done the
obvious -- first I blacklisted bcm43xx, then I installed the driver
that is currently recommended, it didn't work, uninstalled it,
installed the driver I used to use, still no joy. Ndiswrapper -l says
the driver is installed and the hardware is present, but iwconfig says
no wireless.

Oh well. Conan is on and I have class tomorrow. I've been through
ndiswrapper three times before. I'll figure it out. But not tonight. :)

A thousand thanks for everyone's help. :)

Everyone:

   Part of the issues have been resolved. It turns out that despite the
installation instructions, the module must be loaded from within
/etc/mail/spamassassin/FuzzyOcr/FuzzyOcr.cf, not from within spamassassin's
init.pre (or v310.pre).

   Changing the load location removed the warning of not finding the module.

   There are still some remaining issues of configuration options being
ignored, but the author did respond to yesterday's mail to him and I've
asked him about those.

   Now I need to learn from where to load ImageInfo.pm, since it's not
loading from init.pre.

   Thanks all. The reports from folks are that once up and running FuzzyOCR
works well.

Rich

Rich-
A quick google pulled up the following tip:

It looks to me like the newer version of DBD::Pg requires a newer
version of libpq than you have installed.  Now you say that you
have "Postgres 8" installed so you ought to be okay ... but somewhere
on your system there must be an older copy of libpq.so that does not
have that function.

You probably should fix this by changing your ldconfig search path
so that the newer libpq.so is found in preference to the older.


So I'd do a locate libpq.so and see what you have laying around and upgrade
as necessary. Hope this helps :-)

Drew-

Its looks like its returning all instances of /usr/bin/perl  <whatever> can
you invoke setdodoip without referencing perl first? You could always do
something like

pidof /usr/bin/perl setdodoip |awk {'print $1'} which would give you the
correct process id but it might change from time to time so its hard to say
that it would always be correct.

You have '/sbin/pidof ./setdodoip' above. Your script is setdodoip.pl.
I don't know if that makes much difference.

You might experiment with the -x flag, which is supposed to do
"scripts, too" (at least on my system, a quick man pidof should help).

Personally I'd play with drew's suggestion and toss in the script name
like "pidof perl | grep setdodoip\.pl | awk {'print $1'}"

From riznob at gmail.com  Wed Jan 24 10:30:15 2007
From: riznob at gmail.com (Robert Anderson)
Date: Wed, 24 Jan 2007 10:30:15 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] hostnames in sudoers file?
Message-ID: <7cbc132f0701241030j13c6605ch7b98e9a5d0b3314a@mail.gmail.com>

Can someone help me understand why one would use several hostnames in a
sudoers file? It's my understanding that running sudo will only execute
commands locally. In other words, you cannot grant remote sudo permissions
in a local sudoers file....right? My only guess is that it is for the
convenience of managing a single sudoers file that is distributed to several
hosts. If this is the case, is there an easy way to distribute sudoers files
to many hosts, other than simply scp'ing the file? Or is there a way to
"host" a master sudoers file that all the hosts will look to when sudo is
run? Ouch, that sounds like a potential security risk.

Thanks in advance.

-- 
Rob Anderson
riznob at gmail.com

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Wed Jan 24 10:50:20 2007
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Wed, 24 Jan 2007 10:50:20 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] hostnames in sudoers file?
In-Reply-To: <7cbc132f0701241030j13c6605ch7b98e9a5d0b3314a@mail.gmail.com>
References: <7cbc132f0701241030j13c6605ch7b98e9a5d0b3314a@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <1169664620.5088.21.camel@willow.odshp.com>

On Wed, 2007-01-24 at 10:30 -0800, Robert Anderson wrote:
> Can someone help me understand why one would use several hostnames in a
> sudoers file? It's my understanding that running sudo will only execute
> commands locally. In other words, you cannot grant remote sudo permissions
> in a local sudoers file....right? My only guess is that it is for the
> convenience of managing a single sudoers file that is distributed to several
> hosts. If this is the case, is there an easy way to distribute sudoers files
> to many hosts, other than simply scp'ing the file? Or is there a way to
> "host" a master sudoers file that all the hosts will look to when sudo is
> run? Ouch, that sounds like a potential security risk.
> 
> Thanks in advance.

On larger managed networks, configuration files are managed centrally
and then distributed out to the various hosts.  Being able to specify
host-specific options means that you can have one sudoers file that
works across many systems.

As far as means of distribution, there are a number of tools.  Older
home-brewed systems used rdist, rsync, etc.  I am currently using
cfengine[1], although I've been intending to look at puppet[2].  There
are other tools too; see http://www.infrastructures.org for a paper and
discussion.

1. http://www.cfengine.org
2. http://reductivelabs.com/projects/puppet/

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
http://nakedape.cc

On Wed, 24 Jan 2007, drew wymore wrote:

> Unless its changed you don't get a heck of a lot out of shell 
> accounts. No ping and you can compile your own binaries as I recall 
> but its a Solaris box that hosts the shell server so depending on

One great benefit of a shell account is the ability to use rsync over 
ssh to push remote web/ftp content and pull web logs.

Also -- and this is a deep-seated bias that may already or soon be 
outdated -- there's no web-based adminstrative interface that can give 
you the customization power of $EDITOR run from the command line.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

haven't seen many projects follow Agile/XP methods as thoroughly as Luke
has, apparently to much benefit.

<snip>

I'm far from a guru about CUPS, printing or networking. But I noticed
that you are printing PDF files and that piqued my curiosity because I
have problems printing them as well. I always get output, though --
just not the right output.

My printers are Laserjets and they are networked. I have tried sending
PDF files to them directly with lpr and never have a problem getting
output. But I set up the printers in CUPS and make sure I can print a
test page from the CUPS GUI first. Then, when printing with an lpr or
lp command I use the name of the printer as defined in CUPS. E.g., to
print 10 copies of test.pdf to my Laserjet 8000, whose name in CUPS is
"Laserjet-8000-Series-Postscript-(recommended)" I give the command:

lpr -P Laserjet-8000-Series-Postscript-(recommended) -# 10 test.pdf

or

lp -d Laserjet-8000-Series-Postscript-(recommended) -n 10 test.pdf

REP,

   Regardless of whether the extension is in uppercase, it's worked well
before. Ghostview is the filter and it converts the pdf to a ps file before
shipping it off to the printer (which as a PS module installed). If you use
xpdf or acroread, it converts it prior to printing. But, I've not had any
problems printing from the command line before.

> My printers are Laserjets and they are networked. I have tried sending
> PDF files to them directly with lpr and never have a problem getting
> output. But I set up the printers in CUPS and make sure I can print a
> test page from the CUPS GUI first. Then, when printing with an lpr or

> "Laserjet-8000-Series-Postscript-(recommended)" I give the command:

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Wed Jan 24 18:10:04 2007
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Wed, 24 Jan 2007 18:10:04 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Perl/Postgres/SQL-Ledger Problem -- SOLVED!!
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0701241756570.21810@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

   OK, Drew et al., I got the problem fixed.

   To summarize where we left off at the last reel, during my distribution
upgrade a bunch of perl modules went AWOL. Today I grabbed the source for
DBD::Pg-1.49, built, and installed it.

   Then I started looking at the libpq.so libraries and discovered the old
stuff in /usr/local/lib/ I removed them. But, this was after I built
DBD::Pg. That's why when 'ldd Pg.pm' reported using libpq.so.3 I thought
nothing of it. There's a more recent /usr/lib/libpq.so.3.1. That's not the
one to which the perl module pointed.

   So, a repeat performance of 'perl Makefile.PL, make, make test, and make
install' recreated DBD::Pg with the proper library.

   Now, SQL-Ledger lets me log in. Whew!

   Two down, two to go.

Rich

<http://www.appl-ecosys.com>     Voice: 503-667-4517      Fax: 503-667-8863

> DBD::Pg. That's why when 'ldd Pg.pm' reported using libpq.so.3 I thought
> nothing of it. There's a more recent /usr/lib/libpq.so.3.1. That's not the
> one to which the perl module pointed.
>
>    So, a repeat performance of 'perl Makefile.PL, make, make test, and
> make
> install' recreated DBD::Pg with the proper library.
>
>    Now, SQL-Ledger lets me log in. Whew!
>
>    Two down, two to go.
>
> Rich


Thats what I sort of figured was going on. Thats why I suggested  removing
the  module and rebuilding after you found the old crusty
libpq.so.3.xlaying around. Glad you got it nailed Rich!

Drew-

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Wed Jan 24 22:55:59 2007
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Wed, 24 Jan 2007 22:55:59 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] irregular monitor flicker
In-Reply-To: <200701241652.49147.carla@bratgrrl.com>
References: <200701241652.49147.carla@bratgrrl.com>
Message-ID: <20070125065558.GA17255@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Wed, Jan 24, 2007 at 04:52:49PM -0800, Carla Schroder wrote:
> My nice not-very old (about two years old) 19" CRT has developed a problem- it 
> flickers occasionally in an odd way. The screen image shrinks a tiny bit, 
> maybe an eight of an inch, then goes back to normal. Sometimes it does it 
> several times, then stops. There's no regular pattern to it. Is my monitor 
> dying already? The video card is the same age. I checked that its cooling fan 
> works, and the case temperature and cooling are fine. I made sure all cables 
> are snug.  Any ideas?

Dust in the high voltage section of the monitor, causing arcing. 
Internal cleaning fixes that.  It is possible that the problem is
some component failing in the monitor, which will be impossible to fix.

Get it cleaned by a professional if you can;  the high voltage is lethal
and you are too valuable to us to risk.  A TV repair shop will be fine.
If you decide to clean it yourself, be sure to let it sit unplugged a
good long time, surround yourself with non-conductive stuff (rubber
soled shoes), use a big empty bench or table, and keep one hand in your
pocket.  Never ever ever allow a conductive path through your chest
and near your heart.

CRTs are also an implosion hazard, and so you should wear eye
goggles, and have someone around to drag you to the hospital if you
get lacerated by flying glass.  Oh, did I say this was dangerous?
And that I don't want to see you get hurt?

CRTs with power supply problems, or accumulated dust, are fire hazards.
A friend of mine had his house burned down by a flaky monitor;  as
they reconstructed it, parts of the power supply shot three foot
flames.  So attend to it quickly, please - Baked Carla is no more
palatable than Zapped Carla or Lacerated Carla.

Keith
(who is glad that CRTs are slowly becoming extinct)

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993

Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs


From keithl at kl-ic.com  Wed Jan 24 23:06:02 2007
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Wed, 24 Jan 2007 23:06:02 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] A New Issue: Can't Print
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0701241646030.21810@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

Message-ID: <20070125070602.GB17255@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Wed, Jan 24, 2007 at 04:57:41PM -0800, Rich Shepard wrote:
>   This has become a major PITA. I just tried to send a pdf file to the
> printer using lpr. It never came out; when I look at /usr/sbin/printers,
> there it sits in the jobs queue.
...
>   What critical step have I overlooked?

It pings, so it has power and data - if the IP address is correct and
nothing else on the network is stepping on it.  Paper?  Jam?  Toner?  

CUPS needs to render the pdf file to Postscript in order to print.
How big is the file?  Is there enough room in /var and /tmp to do
the work?

Have you tried doing pdf2ps on the file, and printing the resulting
postscript?

A couple days ago, I had a lockup that went away when I removed a very
large file from the job queue, and power cycled the printer (HP4M, BTW).
Perhaps something like that will help you.

Keith

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Wed Jan 24 21:55:10 2007
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (someone)
Date: Wed, 24 Jan 2007 21:55:10 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Was looking for new ISP
In-Reply-To: <e2aeccd60701241436u556120a6ybf5eb6ee60ca73de@mail.gmail.com>
References: <20070124194528.8EFC44432@ulke.nakedape.cc>
	<20070124221525.V99771@eehouse.org>
	<e2aeccd60701241436u556120a6ybf5eb6ee60ca73de@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <1169704511.2783.30.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>


> When I left in 2004 we had emacs and lynx and I'm pretty sure screen. I know
> we didn't have subversion and I highly doubt that they would have installed
> it since then. Shell accounts were given as is without any form of support.
> There wasn't GCC or make but there were the solaris equivalents. I'm
> emailing you some contact info privately to pass along to your sales guy to
> see if they can find out whats available now.
> 
> Drew-

Out of curiosity, why does someone want shell access at a remote 
address on a distant computer to push programs out to instead of 
setting up a web site in house???  This thread doesn't sound 
like the typical idea most people have of what an ISP is.  Opus
Interactive, ISP I go through, doesn't consider doing backup
dns and mail service for my domain let alone shell access standard
service.  I had a hard time getting Opus to forward email addressed
to a private domain into imap accounts it hosts for us in the event
of a DSL outage.  There is no backup if DSL fails in Scappoose 
unless you have Comcast Cable as well and it doesn't go out
simultaneously.  Unlike Beaverton, Opus and the phone company are
it and the phone company tends to take a week to fix problems when 
they arise.  I don't know why it is generally better in Beaverton
when it comes to down time.  Certainly, equating ISP with shell 
access or hosting is definitely different than what ISP means for 
the average Joe.  I know we only have about 2 telephone repair 
people who go out and that we have to share them with other 
communities.  Why is the price of DSL so high compared to cable?
I know overall that you pay less, but on dollar/speed you seem to 
lose even at Comcast's high rates.  Granted, Comcast forces you to
deal with a dynamic ip address and says no to servers.

This reminds me, I've forgotten what our Opus accounts are and am
wondering if my ISP is still a backup or not.  Personally, I think one
is better off working with a buddy on Opus to act as backup than 
asking Opus directly to be the fallback site.

Just because you use a hosting service like godaddy.com etcetera, does
that automatically mean that your hosted site is backed up in case
of a single hosting site outage?  Are you guaranteed that hosting is
better than bringing a T1 directly into your business and running a web
site in house?  How do you trust a 3rd party with your email etcetera?
After all, what is to stop a hosting site employee from
turning into an email thief and selling what they find to the highest
bidder?  How are these hosting sites profitable?  If they are vmware
based, the licenses for that aren't cheap after all.  How do these
so called clean up services that find damaging information on
the Internet about you and have it removed or get it mitigated 
work?

     --  Michael C. Robinson

From eehouse at eehouse.org  Thu Jan 25 10:08:14 2007
From: eehouse at eehouse.org (eehouse)
Date: Thu, 25 Jan 2007 18:08:14 +0000
Subject: [PLUG] Was looking for new ISP
In-Reply-To: <20070125162856.51F124483@ulke.nakedape.cc>;
	from plug-request@lists.pdxlinux.org on Thu, Jan 25, 2007 at
	08:28:56AM -0800
References: <20070125162856.51F124483@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <20070125180813.C99771@eehouse.org>

> Date: Wed, 24 Jan 2007 21:55:10 -0800
> From: someone <plug_0 at robinson-west.com>
> Subject: Re: [PLUG] Was looking for new ISP
> To: "General Linux/UNIX discussion and help;	civil and on-topic"
> 	<plug at lists.pdxlinux.org>
> Message-ID: <1169704511.2783.30.camel at goose.robinson-west.com>
> Content-Type: text/plain
>
> Out of curiosity, why does someone want shell access at a remote 
> address on a distant computer to push programs out to instead of 
> setting up a web site in house??? 

Here are some of my reasons:

* Home DSL is too unreliable to allow me to run a site from home.
  Hell, my DSL modem alone is so flakey I've had to put it on a timer
  resetting it every 24 hours, so the site's guaranteed to go down
  once/day.  (Qwest does not consider a modem that must be
  power-cycled every few days to be broken.)

* On a more general level, I'd rather not sign up for the security
  headaches assocated with running servers on well-known ports, nor do
  I want to pay for the business-grade service plans that permit it.
  I have a LEAF-based router at home, separate DMZ and trusted LAN,
  etc. -- but I'd still rather keep the public stuff somewhere else.

* Many ISPs won't deliver mail to the IP ranges known to belong to DSL
  providers.

* I traval a lot, and read mail from many different places.  With a
  shell account, I run mutt inside screen and keep it running for as
  long as the host stays up.  I disconnect when the plane closes its
  doors and reconnect from work a few hours later and am right back in
  the emacs session I was using to compose a reply.

* I want to use an email address I will always control, and URLs as
  well.  No more ehouse at some.random.isp.com.  Google still finds links
  to one of my sites at my old ISP (which page doesn't exist) ahead of
  the same page where it's been for the past year.

* Once I'm running sites elsewhere, I have to be able to edit and
  debug them in place.  You can't upload a PHP-based site, let alone
  compiled CGI binaries, and expect them to work on a different host.
  I know that's the goal of scripting systems like php, but it's
  imperfectly realized.

What I'm looking for would more accurately be described as a "shell
account with web hosting" than "web hosting with shell access", but
nobody sells shell accounts any more *except* as part of a web hosting
package.  Including peak.org, where I had such an account for many
years.

--Eric

On Thu, 25 Jan 2007, eehouse wrote:

> * Home DSL is too unreliable to allow me to run a site from home. Hell, my
>  DSL modem alone is so flakey I've had to put it on a timer resetting it
>  every 24 hours, so the site's guaranteed to go down once/day.  (Qwest
>  does not consider a modem that must be power-cycled every few days to be
>  broken.)

Ed,

   That's a Qworst issue, not DSL. I have Verizon wire here and it has been
totally reliable for years. Now, the power brick on the Fujitsu modem
croaked a year or so ago, but the Verizon tech loaned me his until I could
buy a replacement. I found one on eBay (whole 'modem') for $18 (with
shipping) that same day; I was the only bidder. Problem solved.

> * On a more general level, I'd rather not sign up for the security
>  headaches assocated with running servers on well-known ports, nor do I
>  want to pay for the business-grade service plans that permit it. I have a
>  LEAF-based router at home, separate DMZ and trusted LAN, etc. -- but I'd
>  still rather keep the public stuff somewhere else.

   I let Aracnet handle the security for my web site and DNS services. They
have full-time experts who do an outstanding job. I run my MTA here, and
with the help of the fine folks who populate the postfix, spamassassin, and
FuzzyOCR mail lists, it's now tuned to grand prix performance. My inbox spam
has dropped to zero the past two days ... except for some UCE from Qwest.

> * Many ISPs won't deliver mail to the IP ranges known to belong to DSL
>  providers.

   Why not register your own domain name and get a static IP address from
your ISP? Then you can use either fetchmail/getmail to suck it down from
their server or you could run your own.

> * I traval a lot, and read mail from many different places.  With a shell
>  account, I run mutt inside screen and keep it running for as long as the
>  host stays up.  I disconnect when the plane closes its doors and
>  reconnect from work a few hours later and am right back in the emacs
>  session I was using to compose a reply.

   I ssh in from any place with free wired or WiFi access and use pine on my
office system from a console on my notebook. Of course, the work I do is
sufficiently non-critical that I don't need to instantly reply to anyone.

> * I want to use an email address I will always control, and URLs as
>  well.  No more ehouse at some.random.isp.com.  Google still finds links
>  to one of my sites at my old ISP (which page doesn't exist) ahead of
>  the same page where it's been for the past year.

   Well, buy your own domain name. Have your ISP of choice point the MX
record to your server.

> * Once I'm running sites elsewhere, I have to be able to edit and debug
>  them in place.  You can't upload a PHP-based site, let alone compiled CGI
>  binaries, and expect them to work on a different host. I know that's the
>  goal of scripting systems like php, but it's imperfectly realized.

   I don't do php, but the couple of cgi scripts I've uploaded to my little
corner of the hosting disk are working just fine.

Rich

There is also the black that is far inferior to CRTs.

I'm hopeful about direct-view LED displays (matrix of LEDs, instead of
LEDs as backlight). Except for resolution, that should resolve all the
lingering issues.
