On Thu, 2007-01-25 at 18:08 +0000, eehouse wrote:
> * Many ISPs won't deliver mail to the IP ranges known to belong to DSL
>   providers.

How do ISPs know which numbers these are and is this public
information?

Opus DSL hasn't been flaky at all.  I have no problem running
a web site in house on their service.

So, if you are a fiber optics customer you are okay?  But how much
do non DSL broadband connections cost?  How many ip addresses are
available that aren't reserved for DSL?

Is the backbone of the Internet IPv6 based now?  Is the kind of
broadband ISP's feed DSL from expensive because of ip address
shortage or other factors?  How much does getting on the large 
fiber optics networks in South Korea touting 20 mb/s connections 
cost per month in U.S. dollars?  Aside from fiber optics being 
rare in the U.S. because you have to run fiber, how has it become 
cost effective in South Korea?

Is piecewise replacement of a POTS system with fiber optics a
cost effective approach to fiber deployment?  What would it take
to build public fiber rings in small cities and interconnect them
to achieve a larger network?

Does ISP blocking of DSL IP blocks negate the usefulness of static
subnets over DSL?  Is the discrimination solely aimed at the dynamic
address pool?  If I'm not mistaken, DSL's theoretical max downstream
speed is 45 megabits per second.  Most of us are lucky if we can 
do 3 mbps/downstream.  I'm on 1.5 mbps/downstream right now. 

I wish the phone system in Scappoose was publicly owned let
alone locally owned.  I do not like CenturyTel.  I do not
understand why we have nationwide telecommunications
mega corporations that are not accountable to their customers.  
It would be awesome to see the establishment of competitive 
publicly owned telephone companies in the Portland Metropolitan 
Area offering Broadband and local and long distance telephone 
service.



Separately on Comcast:

Why is Comcast consistently above the phone company's DSL in 
price?  Comcast is faster, but the company forces it's 
subscribers to pay more per month.  I've asked why they don't 
offer comparable speed to DSL at a competitive price, but I 
didn't get an answer.  Is it true that cable's performance
dwindles in comparison to DSL as more people in your 
community get on it?  Is Comcast trying to cover 
infrastructure costs going to catch up to the phone 
company and lower the price of off promotion service 
to its customers when it does?  

Are cable IP blocks avoided as well by some ISPs?

corporate servers.

Regarding PSU, in engineering departments, they run tons of software
that is designed for Solaris. That's why you see Sun labs.

Oh blah blah blah blah blah.  I call "Bullshit!"  I have had
approximately zero problems with Linux itself.  Just the other day I had
an AIX 5.3 box crash hard because I had the audacity to attach more than
one packet filter at the same time.  IBM had only recently seen the
problem and didn't actually have a public fix yet.

By the time you've tarted up Solaris or AIX (or FreeBSD, for that
matter) to actually be useful, you've added all the free software you
would have gotten for free with a Linux distribution and it's much less
integrated than it is with a Linux distribution.  The biggest cause of
problems I've seen has been stupid administrators (including me, in
years past)--but Linux is only unique in that regard because, within the
last 10 years, it has usually been the first UNIX that many people start
with.

Wil

> information?


DNSRBL's usually .. and yes it is.



Opus DSL hasn't been flaky at all.  I have no problem running
> a web site in house on their service.


It depends on the loop length from the RT (remote terminal) or Central
Office and other characteristics of the line itself. I'd be happy to post
all of them if anyone is interested.


So, if you are a fiber optics customer you are okay?  But how much
> do non DSL broadband connections cost?  How many ip addresses are
> available that aren't reserved for DSL?


I doubt it. I would assume most *consumer* IP ranges regardless of access
type are subject to some penalization but this is just conjecture on my
part.


Is the backbone of the Internet IPv6 based now?  Is the kind of
> broadband ISP's feed DSL from expensive because of ip address
> shortage or other factors?  How much does getting on the large
> fiber optics networks in South Korea touting 20 mb/s connections
> cost per month in U.S. dollars?  Aside from fiber optics being
> rare in the U.S. because you have to run fiber, how has it become
> cost effective in South Korea?


Not really. There is a move to IPv6 but its progressing really slow. Most of
the public networks in SK are subsidized by the government.



Is piecewise replacement of a POTS system with fiber optics a
> cost effective approach to fiber deployment?  What would it take
> to build public fiber rings in small cities and interconnect them
> to achieve a larger network?


Not really, although Verizon is making this happen in their territories
betting on the future (VOIP, Video on Demand ect ect). Its a very signifcant
outlay in capital to replace the aging POTS infrastructure. You'd need
colocation, rights of way from existing telco's or power companies (easiest
from an engineering standpoint for the buildout IMHO) and of course fiber
unless you lease existing dark fiber which is what Google is doing (leasing
existing dark fiber).


Does ISP blocking of DSL IP blocks negate the usefulness of static
> subnets over DSL?  Is the discrimination solely aimed at the dynamic
> address pool?  If I'm not mistaken, DSL's theoretical max downstream
> speed is 45 megabits per second.  Most of us are lucky if we can
> do 3 mbps/downstream.  I'm on 1.5 mbps/downstream right now.


Its generally based on the DHCP pool in *my* experience (results not
typical, your experience may differ) ... The current _deployed_ DSL
infrastructure is capable of 8Mbps down max. DSL2 is slowly being rolled out
by manufactures and deployed by a small subset of providers ... I can't
recall off the top of my head what the max down is, it does increase the
distance from the CO you can be and still receive service though.


I wish the phone system in Scappoose was publicly owned let

From merlyn at stonehenge.com  Fri Jan 26 11:18:45 2007
From: merlyn at stonehenge.com (Randal L. Schwartz)
Date: Fri, 26 Jan 2007 11:18:45 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Re: This article on BSD...
References: <1169752252.2783.37.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
	<45BA43F4.3010909@schluting.com>
Message-ID: <86ac05mwa2.fsf@blue.stonehenge.com>

>>>>> "Charlie" == Charlie Schluting <charlie at schluting.com> writes:

Charlie> We use Solaris, not SunOS by the way. SunOS was BSD, Solaris is
Charlie> System V.

That may have been the casual division, but formally, the release that
included SunOS 4 (BSD-based) was retitled Solaris 1 in retronaming, and SunOS
5 (SysV-based) was titled Solaris 2.  The reason for the casual naming is that
SunOS 4 had never been "sold" under the Solaris 1 name (except on 386
machines, if I recall), and so we sysadmins talked about "sunos" vs "solaris"
to mean version 4 (bsd) vs version 5 (sysv), together with all the respective
changes in the distro.

So it's a SunOS 4 (or 5) operating system and a Solaris 1 (or 2) distro.
Hence you're both right. :)

On Fri, 2007-01-26 at 11:12 -0800, Charlie Schluting wrote:

> Neat!
> I know, I'm a troll. But there is some semblance to reality in what I

And I'm peevish, so let's roll.

> > By the time you've tarted up Solaris or AIX (or FreeBSD, for that
> > matter) to actually be useful, you've added all the free software you
> > would have gotten for free with a Linux distribution and it's much less
> > integrated than it is with a Linux distribution.  
> 
> I really hate that argument. If you want GNU software, then use Linux.
> There's nothing wrong with Linux for things where you want
> tons-o-software. Even web servers (if they're redundant). And the GNU
> package managers for other OS's come with the kitchen sink, so you don't
> have to worry about runtime library linking issues. It's not
> "integrated" but rather helps with some common issues. (blastwave++)

Because userland is important.  I spend most of my life working with
userland, not a kernel directly.  If I'm managing or developing on a
system, I want GNU software, period.  There are few things I hate as
much as being stuck in ksh and having to use standard vi.

But it's not just GNU software--there are certain basic facilities that
are missing or stupid.  Oftentimes trivial stuff, like managing symlinks
in /etc/rc.d/rcN.d (or the lovely Solaris standard, hardlinks).  Do you
know what you have to do to remove from init a static route that was
created with SMIT in AIX? 

 http://nakedape.cc/wiki/PlatformNotes_2fAixNotes section 1.2.4

(And despite the copious pages of documentation written in such a dull
manner that one's brain is tempted to try lycanthropy, not documented.)

SUMA was touted as a killer new tool for automating downloads of
software updates from IBM, but it doesn't fucking work half the time:

http://blogs.ittoolbox.com/emergingtech/fsherman/archives/aix-maintenance-strategies-part-iii-10208

And the ability to find what ports are open by what processes?  Not
without a third party tool (lsof, which recently started segfaulting for
me on all of my AIX systems).  How about a facility for securely
creating temp files with scripts?  Nope, no mktemp.

I hear Solaris 10 is better; I wouldn't know--I did an installation in a
VM and, despite having selected the GNOME desktop, I was unable to login
via X because one of the required GNOME libraries hadn't gotten
installed.  Duh.  I'd planned to fix that, but that was probably 6
months ago.

> I tend to focus on the kernel things (like Linux's broken NFSv4
> implementation; that greatly saddens me. I touted the fact that the OSDL
> tested NFSv4 very well, and they let me down).

NFS sucks; long live NFS.  I haven't mucked with NFSv4 much; my home
directory NFS server at home is still running a 2.4 kernel.  At work, I
limit NFS use to temporary mount points--installation sources (would you
believe that very little of the software we get from IBM actually comes
packaged as an LPP, which makes it impossible to install via NIM?),
database dumps for loading on other systems, etc.  Yes, Linux's
automounter sucks too; I'll give you that.  But Solaris lacks software
RAID and LVM support that can actually be used with the basic system
filesystem.

> True! But I do try to filter those instances out. A kernel panic is a
> kernel panic, no matter what the userspace does.

The only kernel panics I've had with Linux in the last few years were
due to me using non-mainline stuff--like the NVidia and VMware drivers
and trying to rmmod the OpenAFS kernel module.  The ones from farther
back that I can remember were usually due to bad memory and other
hardware errors.

Wil

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Fri Jan 26 12:12:49 2007
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Fri, 26 Jan 2007 12:12:49 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] irregular monitor flicker
In-Reply-To: <200701260232.l0Q2WErB023884@m5p.com>
References: <20070125162856.7C9754484@ulke.nakedape.cc>
	<200701260232.l0Q2WErB023884@m5p.com>
Message-ID: <20070126201248.GA7784@gate.kl-ic.com>

From: Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com>
> CRTs are also an implosion hazard, and so you should wear eye
> goggles, and have someone around to drag you to the hospital if you
> get lacerated by flying glass.  Oh, did I say this was dangerous?
> And that I don't want to see you get hurt?
 
On Thu, Jan 25, 2007 at 06:32:14PM -0800, Elliott Mitchell wrote:
> By Wikipedia's calculations, around 1900 lbs of pressure on the front
> face of a 17" CRT. Maybe PDX should pave roads with CRTs, they seem up
> to the task, unlike the thin asphault used around PDX.
> 
> Also from Wikipedia, apparently earlier CRTs could rupture around the
> neck and propel the electron guns through the faceplate. Must of been an
> /interesting/ experience.

The glass around the electron gun ("the neck") is thin compared to the
faceplate, which must be thick for safety, radiation, and mechanical
stability reasons.  The neck glass is thinner partly to place the
deflection yoke closer to the electron beam, but the main reason is
that consumers aren't normally exposed to it;  better to put extra
glass in the faceplate rather than the neck.  The weakest place is
the evacuation stem in the center of the pins, and breaking that is
pretty safe.  But the glass weld from the neck to the bell of the CRT
is weak and under high stress, and thicker glass just makes it worse
(more cooling mismatch after the weld).  This is typically where a
CRT breaks, and breaking throws very sharp shards.  Most go inwards,
but the mechanics of chaotic rapid fracturing will send some of the
shards in all directions.

When the monitor case is intact, the user is protected from the weld
by the yoke, the electronics assemblies to the side, and the outer
case.  While it is unlikely that the glass at the weld will shatter,
it can and does, and I have broken CRTs this way.  I used to work
at Tektronix, where they made CRTs, and have seen the safety and
slow-motion testing movies, and had friends in the engineering glass
shop who made CRTs.  The back side of a CRT is not as dangerous as
dynamite, probably no more dangerous than an M80 wrapped in broken
glass, but that can still lacerate or blind you.  Personally, when I
work inside monitors I risk hand wounds (they heal), but I NEVER work
with the case off without full-enclosure eye protection.  This is
reality, not a game, and you can't start over if killed or maimed.

Elliott, you may like CRT monitors a lot, but when you clean one,
don't risk a Darwin award attempting to prove your invulnerability.
CRTs in dusty places need to be cleaned for safety reasons - so do
desktop PC power supplies.  Some people here may not have the money
to get their CRT monitors cleaned professionally, and I would not
want their maiming to be on my conscience.  YMMV, of course.

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

> glass in the faceplate rather than the neck.  The weakest place is
> the evacuation stem in the center of the pins, and breaking that is
> pretty safe.  But the glass weld from the neck to the bell of the CRT
> is weak and under high stress, and thicker glass just makes it worse
> (more cooling mismatch after the weld).  This is typically where a
> CRT breaks, and breaking throws very sharp shards.  Most go inwards,
> but the mechanics of chaotic rapid fracturing will send some of the
> shards in all directions.

>From my read of the article they were refering to complete fractures
around the neck causing air pressure to propelling the electron guns
forward with sufficient force to go completely through the faceplate. I
would not of wanted to be in front if that happened. Might of been
interesting to witness, from several yards away.

> Elliott, you may like CRT monitors a lot, but when you clean one,
> don't risk a Darwin award attempting to prove your invulnerability.
> CRTs in dusty places need to be cleaned for safety reasons - so do
> desktop PC power supplies.  Some people here may not have the money
> to get their CRT monitors cleaned professionally, and I would not
> want their maiming to be on my conscience.  YMMV, of course.

I like CRTs for display. I said nothing of liking cleaning them. Even
without the reminder I wouldn't try it. Thanks for the reminder of why
though.


--

Jenifer,

You are using hardware RAID. You can go into the Smart Array  
utilities at boot to see how they are mirrored, but the default for  
those servers with two disks I think is to mirror them. Thats why you  
are only seeing one disk. Its a mirrored hardware array.

Doug

                             MEETING ANNOUNCEMENT

                        The Portland Linux/Unix Group
                                  will meet
                          7 PM Thursday Feb 1, 2007
                                      at
                          Portland State University
                                      in
                            Smith Memorial Center
                                   Room 294
                           On the block bounded by
       SW Montgomery, SW Broadway (7th), SW Harrison, and SW Park (9th)

      *******************************************************************

                                 PRESENTATION

                     Adventures in Computer Non-Security

                                      by

                                 David Mandel
                            <dmandel at pdxLinux.org>


      Most system users and administrators aren't security experts
      and don't want to be; but they have to deal with security
      in order to get other things done.  And they have to do this
      within very tight budgets for both in terms of time and money.

      David Mandel fits this situation.

      He has had a number of systems invaded over the years including
      two in the last year.  David will describe a number of techniques
      he uses and/or recommends to protect systems and data on
      the systems; and where these techniques have worked and where
      they have failed.  He will also discuss tools that have proven
      useful in examining invasions and repairing the damage.

      *******************************************************************

          Agenda:

              7:00 - 7:30  Business
                   We will discuss the status of our ongoing projects
                   including PLUG's monthly Advanced Topics meetings,
                   PLUG's monthly hands on clinics, PLUG for Education,
                   etc.

              7:30 - 8:30  Presentation

                   See above

From johnxj at comcast.net  Sat Jan 27 22:12:31 2007
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Sat, 27 Jan 2007 22:12:31 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Permissions, logic, philosophy and pragmatics
Message-ID: <20070127221231.acfdcdff.johnxj@comcast.net>

Having recently upgraded to Edgy and reinstalled ndiswrapper (always
gets broke on an upgrade), I found that Ubuntu had decided that System
> Administration > Networking worked entirely too well for a Linux 
distribution. Therefore, it was necessary to screw it up so it no
longer works properly. In addition to changing the buttons on the
dialog box, it no longer automatically senses available networks.

As a result I found a little applet called wifi-radar that works even
better than the old built in System > Administration > Networking GUI
window. By "better" I mean it launches instantly, finds available
wireless networks in seconds, and connects to the one I choose in a few
seconds more. The now-broken GUI used to make me stare at an hourglass
for several minutes to do the same thing.

However, like the old GUI, wifi-radar requires root login to launch it.
Now, I have always had to do that with the old system too, so that is
nothing new. However, it got me thinking about this issue. Like, why?

Consider that upon booting the computer, eth0, the wired network
connection, is readily available to anyone without additional root
login. So anyone who can log into the computer can just hook up an RJ45
cable and proceed to download all my secrets. OK, I don't have any
secrets on this computer, but the point is that there is no real
security other than the need to know my username and password to log in
after booting. So evidently Ubuntu thinks that access to eth0 should be
granted to anyone who can log in. Then why not access to wlan0? What's
the diff?

Wifi-radar comes with instructions for using PAM to launch it as a
normal user. I don't know what PAM is, but I can probably figure it out
and follow the instructions. But before I go to the trouble, does my
logic make sense? 


From drew.kalina at gmail.com  Sat Jan 27 23:14:47 2007

PAM - pluggable authentication modules.  It provides an interface for
authentication to programs.

http://www.kernel.org/pub/linux/libs/pam/
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pluggable_Authentication_Modules

What do they ask you to do?

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sun Jan 28 07:28:06 2007
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sun, 28 Jan 2007 07:28:06 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] OT: Win-XP Guest Account Network File Access (samba)
In-Reply-To: <B0030369102@bct-mail.bctonline.com>
References: <B0030369102@bct-mail.bctonline.com>
Message-ID: <20070128152806.GA7518@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Sat, Jan 27, 2007 at 05:37:02PM -0800, Charles Sliger wrote:
> 
> Does anyone know how to get the "Guest" account on a Win-XP system to access
> a remote filesystem?
> 
> The remote filesystem in question is a Linux/Samba box.
> 
> The Win-XP "Admin" account can see and access the remote files just fine.
> 
> The Win-XP "Guest" account can see the local net under "Microsoft Windows
> Network" but cannot see the systems.


I have never run WinXP, but I am guessing that Win2K is similar.
I have Win2K running under VMware (a licensed copy, BSA code cops!)
and talking to the Linux host with Samba.  I had a problem like yours.

I suspect you have not entered the appropriate name/password combination
for the guest account into the remote system's samba password file. 
Read the man page for "smbpasswd" on the remote system. 

If that doesn't work, then I have no idea.  I don't run spyware-by-design
operating systems like WinXP and Win2K3.  You should ask Microsoft, since
they know what you are doing and are sniggering at your missteps now. :-/

Keith

So that no password is required.. here's an example:

[Myth Recordings]
   browseable = yes
   path = /stuff/mythtv
   public = yes
   writable = no
   guest ok = yes

On 1/27/07, John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> wrote:
>
> On a more serious note, I might go for the idea of the rotating cube
> for the various workspaces. But my computer doesn't have a touch
> screen. Can I make it rotate just with the mouse?



Yes. In Beryl (compiz probably as well) ctl + alt + mouse click you can drag
the cube in all directions.. you can also go to the top of the cube and
rotate it then down to the other desktops with only the keyboard in Beryl
with ctl + alt + arrow keys.. but it doesn't go 3D with the complete nicey
nice rotation..

What's interesting as well is that the cube is thought of as one desktop. A
desktop with 4 usable sides (workspaces) and a top in the bottom. Add a
desktop in the configuration you end up with two separate desktops which are
each their own cube of 4 workspaces.

killer really,

You threw me for a bit, but then I noticed the "onyx" in your prompt
and tried ssh'ing to it from my virtual server.  That worked.
Apparently a standard shell account is included in the price of "Web
hosting".  So I've set mutt up there to fetch my mail via pop (no
imap...), and as far as I can tell everything works.  I'm posting this
from there now.

The shell account machines are pretty similar to the virtual servers,
only with lots more software installed, so I was able to compile a cgi
app there and move it over to be hosted.  My most demanding cgi is a
dictionary compressor for my handheld FOSS word game (see .sig), and
hosted on SpiritOne execution time's dropped from ~15 seconds to
instantaneous (as experienced by a user.)  So I'm one happy camper.

Thanks to everybody who pointed me at these guys.

>>>>> "Rich" == Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> writes:

Rich>    What might make Date::Calc and a couple of its prerequsites
Rich> suddenly disappear?

Rich>    Last night -- at 00:01 and 04:40 -- the pflogsumm mail log
Rich> analyzer ran and mailed me the report. I just tried running it
Rich> manually to get some sample data from the report, but the
Rich> attempt failed because Date::Calc was not found.

Rich>    This is the second time in a few months this has
Rich> happened. I'm wondering what it could be.

The environment in which it ran.  That is, the $PATH or equivalent may
be different between the context in which your mid-night cron job ran,
and the context in which you ran it manually.  That seems the most
likely explanation.

> 
> By Wikipedia's calculations, around 1900 lbs of pressure on the front
> face of a 17" CRT. Maybe PDX should pave roads with CRTs, they seem up
> to the task, unlike the thin asphault used around PDX.
>

> neck and propel the electron guns through the faceplate. Must of been an
> /interesting/ experience.
> 
> > Keith
> > (who is glad that CRTs are slowly becoming extinct)
> 
> Except the modern LCDs still don't compete on resolution. Up to 20" LCDs
> tend to be 1280x1024 resolution, while at a mere 19" (equiv to 18" LCD)
> most CRTs can do 1600x1200 (and I believe some 17" ones could do that).
> There is also the black that is far inferior to CRTs.
> 
> I'm hopeful about direct-view LED displays (matrix of LEDs, instead of
> LEDs as backlight). Except for resolution, that should resolve all the
> lingering issues.
> 
> 

LCD flat panels can be high resolution, but they aren't generally and
it's not because of any technological barrier.  Look at laptops,
1400x1050 or better in a  15.1 inch screen, yet try to get that in a
flat panel.  You'll either find you can't or that it is very expensive.
1280x1024 in 1 19" viewable 20" LCD panel is far less than what is
technologically possible.  I think the resolution of flat panels tends
to be low because companies are of the opinion that people are more
concerned about cost than they are about performance.  It is odd to me
though that the average laptop monitor is higher resolution than the
average flat screen.

     --  Michael Robinson

From sofar at foo-projects.org  Tue Jan 30 11:09:06 2007
From: sofar at foo-projects.org (Auke Kok)
Date: Tue, 30 Jan 2007 11:09:06 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Whois and dnsreports.com...
In-Reply-To: <20070129220101.7wa1btv6o0sg00kk@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
References: <20070129220101.7wa1btv6o0sg00kk@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <45BF97D2.6050707@foo-projects.org>

plug_0 at robinson-west.com wrote:
> discrepancy.
> 
> If I do whois robinson-west.com I get that the third nameserver is
> ns2.opusnet.com which has an ip address of 209.210.200.3.
> 
> Now then, dnsreports.com tells me that the third dns server is at
> 209.210.200.7.  So my question is, why does it say that?
> I may have to contact my ISP and ask what's going on with that.

1) whois != DNS

2) your nameservers are glued to an IP in the TLD ns for your domain (in this 
case the TLD for .com). This is a 'glue record'.

3) when you register your NS servers you provide a name, but the TLD ns needs 
both name and IP for this glue. it gets the IP from the maintainer of the NS 
server, who has the authority to change it.

It seems like that might have happened. allthough strange, this happens from 
time to time if you use other peoples dns services.

$ dig -t NS robinson-west.com. @209.210.200.7

; <<>> DiG 9.3.3 <<>> -t NS robinson-west.com. @209.210.200.7
; (1 server found)
;; global options:  printcmd
;; connection timed out; no servers could be reached

that's _not_ good. but I don't see this IP in your dnsreport.com. perhaps they 
just changed something?

Auke

From rsteff at comcast.net  Tue Jan 30 14:21:24 2007
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Tue, 30 Jan 2007 14:21:24 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] PIcture
In-Reply-To: <727fc2ee0701292246t46becedv69d49863624548c4@mail.gmail.com>
References: <727fc2ee0701292246t46becedv69d49863624548c4@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <45BFC4E4.3080801@comcast.net>

User Iam wrote:

> When I import my pictures from the camera they are 700K...
> 
> How do I make them thumbnail size???   <17K>

I use convert (included with ImageMagik) to create smaller images from
the ones I download from my camera. I happen to make 800 x 600 images,
but you could change the 800 to the larger dimension of the thumbnail
size you want and get the same thing.

In the directory where I've downloaded the images, I create a directory
named 800. Then I run the following shell script:

------------------------------------------------------------------------
#!/bin/bash
for i in *jpg;
do
convert -size 800x800 -resize 800x800 $i 800/`basename $i .jpg`-800.jpg;
done
------------------------------------------------------------------------

Note that for the script to work, the extension on the image files has
to be jpg, and can't be JPG. If your camera doesn't have a setting for
lower case file names, you'll have to convert them yourself, first. If
that's a concern, let us know. Somewhere, I still have the notes that
told me the three step process for renaming a batch of files.

800x800 produces images that are either 800x600 or 600x800, depending on
which way I held the camera.


Also, see:

http://imagemagick.sourceforge.net/http/www/convert.html

for another example that describes making 120x120 thumbnails.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

I suspect a module or library or something that OOo 2.04 depends on is
either missing or messed up. I have come to this conclusion after
extensive querying the OOo e-list and forums, as well as the Ubuntu
forums, plus googling and searching everything I can find. 

The problem: Upgrading Dapper amd64 to Edgy amd64 also upgraded OOo
2.02 to 2.04. Everything works fine except drag and drop editing of
text; i.e., select a sentence in the middle of a paragraph and drag it
to the beginning of the paragraph. This has always worked fine since
forever with OOo, but now nothing happens. It just doesn't work at all.
No error messages, no crashes; just nothing happens. The only thing I
found was a post some months ago to an Ubuntu forum, and evidently it
affected only two people. No solution was ever posted. I queried the
two individuals privately and one responded that he never found a
solution. The problem affects only OOo; I can drag and drop text fine
in AbiWord, etc.

I'd go back to OOo 2.02, except with the Edgy repositories the only
version that appears in Synaptic is 2.04. In fact, I can't even find
2.02 on openoffice.org. I have reinstalled OOo 2.04 via Synaptic,
including all the individual components, but no change. However, there
are undoubtedly things that it uses and depends on that are not in the
open- alphabetical listing, so I would not see them. I searched all
over openoffice.org, but this information is apparently a state secret.
I am wondering if there is a way via the command line (or any other
way) to determine what libraries, modules and dependencies exist for a
program. Any other suggestions also welcome, of course.

> to the beginning of the paragraph. This has always worked fine since
> forever with OOo, but now nothing happens. It just doesn't work at all.

> found was a post some months ago to an Ubuntu forum, and evidently it
> affected only two people. No solution was ever posted. I queried the
> two individuals privately and one responded that he never found a
> solution. The problem affects only OOo; I can drag and drop text fine
> in AbiWord, etc.
>
> I'd go back to OOo 2.02, except with the Edgy repositories the only
> version that appears in Synaptic is 2.04. In fact, I can't even find
> 2.02 on openoffice.org. I have reinstalled OOo 2.04 via Synaptic,
> including all the individual components, but no change. However, there
> are undoubtedly things that it uses and depends on that are not in the
> open- alphabetical listing, so I would not see them. I searched all
> over openoffice.org, but this information is apparently a state secret.
> I am wondering if there is a way via the command line (or any other
> way) to determine what libraries, modules and dependencies exist for a
> program. Any other suggestions also welcome, of course.


ldd `which <binary>` will give you a list of all shared modules that any
given binary depends on.

From dmandel at pdxLinux.org  Wed Jan 31 01:12:44 2007

                             MEETING ANNOUNCEMENT

                        The Portland Linux/Unix Group
                                  will meet
                          7 PM Thursday Feb 1, 2007
                                      at
                          Portland State University
                                      in
                            Smith Memorial Center
                                   Room 294
                           On the block bounded by
       SW Montgomery, SW Broadway (7th), SW Harrison, and SW Park (9th)

      *******************************************************************

                                 PRESENTATION

                     Adventures in Computer Non-Security

                                      by

                                 David Mandel
                            <dmandel at pdxLinux.org>


      Most system users and administrators aren't security experts
      and don't want to be; but they have to deal with security
      in order to get other things done.  And they have to do this
      within very tight budgets for both in terms of time and money.

      David Mandel fits this situation.

      He has had a number of systems invaded over the years including
      two in the last year.  David will describe a number of techniques
      he uses and/or recommends to protect systems and data on
      the systems; and where these techniques have worked and where
      they have failed.  He will also discuss tools that have proven
      useful in examining invasions and repairing the damage.

      *******************************************************************

          Agenda:

              7:00 - 7:30  Business
                   We will discuss the status of our ongoing projects
                   including PLUG's monthly Advanced Topics meetings,
                   PLUG's monthly hands on clinics, PLUG for Education,
                   etc.

              7:30 - 8:30  Presentation

                   See above

              9:00 - ...  Beer
                                  The Lucky Labrador Brewing Company
                                  915 SE Hawthorne

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Wed Jan 31 14:19:14 2007
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Wed, 31 Jan 2007 14:19:14 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Small form factor PCs
Message-ID: <20070131221914.GA20149@gate.kl-ic.com>


Attempting to conserve desk space in my wife's tiny office, about
a year ago I purchased a Shuttle ST62K micro-ATX computer.  It is
small and cute, but the fan has failed three times now (many clever
but futile fixes, long story) and the damned thing is just too
unreliable to maintain remotely.

Any recommendations for a reliable small form factor X86 system
to replace this P.O.S.?   It must be compatable with Linux,
needless to say, and I hope it can use the same CD and 5.25
hard drive and DIMMs as the old machine, so I can gut the
beast before sending it to freegeek for scrap.  It will need
either a DVI video connector, or a slot for a low-power DVI
video card.

Keith

Rich Shepard said the following on 01/31/2007 03:24 PM:
>   Has anyone here experience with Ekiga (formerly GnomeMeeting)? I'd 
> like to
> learn what's needed to use it and what experiences folks have had using it
> ... particularly when the other end(s) are running Microsoft OSes.

> Rich
> 

All you need is to register for a free http://ekiga.net account and have 
a microphone and speakers... or a USB headset (e.g. Logitech 350).  I 
have found that the sound quality is not as good as skype, but it is 
open source!

You can also use it to call toll free numbers for free using a syntax 
of: sip:*1800________ at fwd.pulver.com in the dialing box.

John


From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Wed Jan 31 16:18:09 2007
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Wed, 31 Jan 2007 16:18:09 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Small form factor PCs
In-Reply-To: <20070131221914.GA20149@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <20070131221914.GA20149@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <200701311618.10316.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from Keith Lofstrom
# on Wednesday 31 January 2007 02:19 pm:

>I hope it can use the same CD and 5.25 hard drive

You didn't really put a quantum bigfoot in a shuttle case, did you?  
Maybe that's a 3.5" drive?

Anyhow, the mac mini is certainly quiet, but afaik, hasn't got a bios 
(and good luck finding the hood latch.)

Aopen has a mini knock-off with (IIRC) an epia motherboard.  Not sure 
about reliability though.  Also, the price is not much below the mac 
mini, though I guess without the gigabit ethernet and bluetooth, etc.

Assuming you don't need huge processing power or extremely small 
footprint, you might look for a refurb ibm desktop.  I've got a P133, 
and of the boxen I've had from that era, it is the only one which is 
not yet flaky.  I opened it a while ago to dust it out (after 400 days 
uptime as a failover-only machine (before that, probably just as much 
uptime as the web+svn server)) and found that the front fan (which was 
still spinning fine, yet silently) had lost a blade.  Now, plastic 
should be expected to fail, but the fact that I heard not a peep of 
complaint from the bearings is a testament to the quality of such 
insignificant details (you know, the sort that mean the difference 
between flaky and not.)

--Eric

> > either a DVI video connector, or a slot for a low-power DVI
> > video card.
>
>
I have a suggestion though I have not used either of these myself yet.

Acer and HP have "book" form factor (as opposed to "lunch box" like the
shuttle and clones or Mac Mini) micro mini-tower systems.
They come in low end (Celeron) and mid-range (Core 2 Duo).  They use
Mini-ITX or some similar small form factor motherboard,
laptop size DDR, but desktop size (5.25) optical drive and (3.5) hard
drive.  The power supply is laptop brick style instead of inside the case.
Rather nice looking unit, but I can't speak for reliability.

HP:
<
http://www.shopping.hp.com/webapp/shopping/computer_can_series.do?storeName=computer_store&category=desktops&series_name=s7700y_series&tab_switch=true&a1=Usage&v1=Entertainment+and+multimedia&tab=overview
>

Acer:
<
http://us.acer.com/acereuro/page4.do?sp=page4&dau22.oid=21636&UserCtxParam=0&GroupCtxParam=0&dctx1=25&CountryISOCtxParam=US&LanguageISOCtxParam=en&ctx3=-1&ctx4=United+States&crc=4256645324
>

As far as I can see the Acer has DVI out while the HP does not.

I will put in my $.02 about Shuttle stuff being sub-par.

On 1/31/07, Eric Wilhelm <scratchcomputing at gmail.com> wrote:
> Anyhow, the mac mini is certainly quiet, but afaik, hasn't got a bios
> (and good luck finding the hood latch.)

With the "Boot Camp" firmware updates it has a legacy BIOS mode
allowing grub to work (or use rEFIt, grub2, or elilo???).

Apparently, the Minis are among the best of the Intel Macs at Linux.
It's just an Intel chipset, Intel video, etc. It has Atheros wireless,
so you'd need to do Madwifi or sort out something else there.

FYI - Thought you all might be interested..

    Ubuntu Live
    July 22-24, 2007
    Oregon Convention Center
    Portland, Oregon
    http://www.ubuntulive.com
    Ubuntu Live Is Coming in July! Share Your Knowledge and Enthusiasm as a
    Speaker - Submit Your Proposal Now.
    Ubuntu Live is coming to Portland, Oregon, July 22-24. It's the first
    official gathering of the Ubuntu community - welcoming experts, longtime
    users, established companies, and newcomers alike to an energizing
    environment for sharing, learning, and exploring all things Ubuntu.
    Co-presented by O'Reilly Media and Canonical Ltd., the inaugural
    conference will offer expert-led tutorials, big-picture plenary
    gatherings, focused sessions, and a lively "hallway track" that promise to
    bring the varied participants in the Ubuntu community face to face.
    Ubuntu Live will gather IT professionals, government and business leaders,
    educators, community leaders, and enterprise users. We want your big ideas
    and enlightening experiences, what works and what doesn't, hands-on
    techniques, best practices, and bold visions for the future. We will aim

- Ignored:
    to address and help resolve the issues faced by those in the trenches, and
    we welcome your submissions on all aspects of developing, using,
    customizing, and deploying Ubuntu. The possibilities are endless, but
    might include:
    
    - Using Ubuntu at the edge of the network
    - Ubuntu in small and medium businesses
    - Ubuntu in the NGO/non-profit sector
    - High performance computing
    - Ubuntu Community contributions
    - Ubuntu use in developing economies
    - Ubuntu in education
    - Building Ubuntu-based appliances and products
    - Point of sale, mobile, scientific research, banking, and other
    verticals
    - Performance optimization in Ubuntu
    - Ubuntu derivatives, such as Kubuntu, Edubuntu, and Xubuntu
    
    Proposals are due no later then February 14 11:59 p.m. (PST). Find further
    guidelines and information, as well as the proposal submission form, at
    http://www.ubuntulive.com/cs/ubuntu/create/e_sess
    
    Be sure to save the dates--July 22-24--for this ground-breaking meeting
    which will overlap with OSCON, the O'Reilly Open Source Convention, to
    offer you even greater exposure to the open source community. Registration
    will open in April. Sign up now for the conference newsletter to receive
    news and updates as preparations progress for Ubuntu Live.
    http://www.oreillynet.com/cs/nl/home#conferences
    (Note: Login required)
    
    Ubuntu Live promises to embrace the essence of its name the idea that "I
    am who I am because of who we all are" or "humanity to others" - with the
    goal of each participant leaving the conference enlightened, enriched, and
    inspired.
    
    We hope to see you in Portland!

From tim at wescottdesign.com  Wed Jan 31 19:41:27 2007
From: tim at wescottdesign.com (Tim Wescott)

Subject: [PLUG] Laptop Recommendations
Message-ID: <eprnh8$qb4$1@sea.gmane.org>

Its time to retire my tired old Dell laptop, and I'm planning on 
replacing it with one running Linux.  If possible, I'd like to either 
get one that either doesn't have an OS installed and put Linux on it, or 
buy one that comes with Linux preloaded.

Web searching has shown me that Linux Certified (www.linuxcertified.com) 
has Lenovo laptops with Linux already loaded.

Has anyone had any experiences with this place?  Does anyone have any 
recommendations for an alternate?  Are there any laptops, or laptop 
manufacturers that you've had particularly good (or bad) experiences with?

Thanks.

-- 
Tim Wescott
Wescott Design Services
www.wescottdesign.com

Thumbs up to Lenovo. I've also had luck with Acer and HP. But the HP
required use of ndis-wrapper to get the wireless working whereas I could use
the Madwifi drivers on the Acer. My recommendation would be to look at the
hardware on the laptop and ensure it has an open source driver or a well
supported  commercial driver such as the one's that Nvidia releases.

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Wed Jan 31 00:03:58 2007
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (plug_0 at robinson-west.com)
Date: Wed, 31 Jan 2007 00:03:58 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] hard drive geometry...
Message-ID: <20070131000358.cmp371of408o4kwc@xerxes.robinson-west.com>

Back around 1995 or so, we started to see geometry
translating hard drives.

I'm wondering why the registers that the old CHS standard
depended on weren't increased in size?  Where an 8 bit
register was used, replace that with a 16 bit register,
etcetera.

I've received a comment that the register size can't be changed
because: dos, Windows 95, and every other OS from that far back
will break.  Someone also decided to throw in anti Linux fud by
commenting that BSD is better because it isn't "cobbled" together
with third party software, mostly from GNU, but I'm digressing.

Doubling the size of the respective registers so that CHS will
still work today with our larger hard drives seems to me to be
a more straightforward solution.  I guess I don't see where the
software problems lie.  Is it the size of on processor registers
that are creating the: 16 heads, 1023 cylinders, 63 sectors
limitation?  Are these registers in memory?

Hard drive translation is extremely confusing to me.  I find
it a very hard topic to understand.  Maybe I just lack a good
explanation that I can follow.

Beyond hard drive geometry discussions, the idea that partition hiding
is a bad hack was proposed.  Is the idea of hiding fat partitions
really a hack?  I think there is a special partition type for hidden
fat.  XP and 2000, perhaps NT too, don't seem to honor hidden fat.

With Vista only running on machines that are less than a year old, Microsoft
could demand a change to the way hard drives are accessed on PC's.   
Microsoft doesn't have to worry about Vista being run on computers  
before the change
is made, because it won't run on them.  Sucking up processor power just
to impress me visually doesn't make an operating system better in my
opinion.  From what I've heard, it sounds like Microsoft likes the OEM
channel and that Vista will give OEM hell a whole new meaning.

Do Apple computers have far less legacy support than PCs in order to
keep them simple and theoretically produce a more stable platform?

      --  Michael C. Robinson
